Classification of Blood and Its Impurity in Hanafi Fiqh
Initial ruling on various types of blood and their legal purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify types of blood as pure (tahir) or impure (najis) according to Hanafi fiqh, and state the exceptions for martyr's blood, non-flowing blood, and insect blood.

Core Idea
Not all blood is impure; only flowing blood is najis, but martyr's blood and insect blood are exempt.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between types of blood based on flow and source. This lesson examines the key ruling and its exceptions, grounded in the principle of pardon ('afw) for unavoidable impurities. Understanding these categories is essential for ensuring prayer is valid when blood is present on the body or clothing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on blood
The default ruling: flowing blood (dam masfuh) is impure (najis). This is the general principle from which the exceptions are carved out.
The passage opens by stating that 'blood' in the matn refers to the poured blood (dam masfuh).
2
Recognize the exception for the martyr
The blood of a martyr (shahid) while on his body is pure. This is a special concession so that funeral prayer and other acts can be performed without removing blood.
Once separated from the body, it becomes impure like other blood.
3
Understand the concept of 'flowing'
Only blood that flows or pours out is impure. Residual blood in meat, veins, liver, or spleen is not flowing and therefore not impure.
The ruling hinges on whether the blood is 'sail' (flowing) or not.
4
Examine the dispute on heart blood
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the blood of a sheep's heart: some sources say it is pure like liver/spleen, others say impure. This is a minor difference with no settled position.
The mufta bihi is not explicitly stated, but the prevailing view leans toward purity due to similarity to liver.
5
Apply the principle of pardon to insect blood
Blood of bedbugs, fleas, and lice is excused (ma'fu) even if abundant, because avoiding it is extremely difficult.
This is based on the maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراد بالدم الدم المسفوح غير دم الشهيد فخرج الدم الباقي في اللحم المهزول إذا قطع والباقي في العروق، والدم الذي في الكبد الذي يكون مكمناً فيه لا ما كان من غيره وأما دم قلب الشاة ففي روضة الناطفي أنه طاهر كدم الكبد والطحال وفي القنية إنه نجس وقيل طاهر. وخرج الدم الذي لم يسل من بدن الإنسان كما سيأتي ودم البق والبراغيث والقمل وإن كثر ودم على ما سيأتي. ودخل دم الحيض والنفاس والاستحاضة وكل دم أوجب الوضوء أو الغسل، ودم الحلمة والوزغ وقيده في الظهيرية بأن يكون سائلاً. وفي المحيط : ودم الحلمة نجس وهي ثلاثة أنواع : قراد وحمنانة وحلمة، فالقراد أصغر أنواعه، والحمنانة أوسطها وليس لهما دم سائل والحلمة أكبرها ولها دم سائل، ودم كل عرق نجس، وكذا الدم السائل من سائر الحيوانات. وأما دم الشهيد فهو طاهر ما دام عليه فإذا أبين منه كان نجساً. كذا في الظهيرية، حتى لو حمله ملطخاً به في الصلاة صحت.
He intended by blood: the poured blood other than the blood of the martyr. So excluded is the blood remaining in lean meat when cut and the blood remaining in veins, and the blood in the liver that is stored therein, not other than that. As for the blood of a sheep's heart, in Rawdat al-Natifi it is pure like the blood of the liver and spleen, and in al-Quniyah it is impure, and it is said pure. And excluded is the blood that has not flowed from the human body as will come, and the blood of bedbugs, fleas, and lice even if abundant, and blood upon what will come. And included is menstrual blood, postnatal blood, and istihadah blood, and every blood that necessitates wudu or ghusl, and the blood of the tick and the lizard, and it was restricted in al-Zahiriyyah to that which is flowing. And in al-Muhit: the blood of the tick is impure, and it is three types: qarad, himmananah, and halmah. Qarad is the smallest type, himmananah is the middle and they have no flowing blood, and halmah is the largest and it has flowing blood. And the blood of every vein is impure, and likewise the flowing blood from all animals. As for the blood of the martyr, it is pure as long as it remains on him; if it becomes separated from him, it becomes impure. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah, so that if he carries him smeared with it in prayer, it is valid.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the classification of blood impurities. The author defines that 'blood' in the matn refers to flowing blood (dam masfuh), excluding the blood of the martyr while on his body. It then lists various exceptions: blood left in meat or veins, blood in liver/spleen/heart (disputed), blood of insects (bedbugs, fleas, lice) which is excused due to difficulty of avoidance. Included in the ruling are menstrual, postnatal, and istihadah blood, and any blood that necessitates ablution. The blood of ticks and lizards is impure if flowing. The blood of the martyr is pure as long as it remains on his body, a concession so that his funeral prayer can be performed.

Key Terms
دم مسفوح (dam masfuh): Blood that flows or is poured out, as opposed to blood that remains in meat or organs.
دم الشهيد (dam al-shahid): The blood of a martyr who died in battle; it is ruled pure while on his body.
عفا عنه (ufiya 'anhu): Forgiveness/exemption due to difficulty of avoidance (mashaqqa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which blood is considered pure (tahir) as long as it remains on the body?
A) The blood of a martyr
B) Menstrual blood
C) Blood of bedbugs
D) Blood of a sheep's heart

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, only flowing blood is impure, with specific exceptions for the martyr's blood (pure on his body) and insect blood (excused due to hardship).

General Rulings on Urine in the Hanafi School
General exposition of urine impurity rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi classification of urine from humans, edible animals, and bats, and apply the concepts of light impurity (takhfif) and excused impurity (ma'fu).

Core Idea
Urine is generally impure, but bat urine is pure due to necessity, and edible animal urine is light impurity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi ruling on urine of various animals. While urine is generally impure (najis), the school makes exceptions based on the animal's edibility and the practicality of avoidance. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for daily taharah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling for urine
The passage begins by stating that 'urine' in the matn covers all urine, whether human or animal. The default ruling is impurity (najasah).
2
Recognize the first exception: bat urine
Bat urine is explicitly excluded and ruled pure (tahir). The reason given is ta'adhur al-ihtiraz (impossibility of avoidance) due to bats frequenting places.
This is a concession for necessity (darurah).
3
Recognize the second exception: urine of edible animals
Urine of animals whose meat is eaten is classified as light impurity (takhfif). This means it does not automatically spoil water or clothing like heavy impurity.
Light impurity (najis khafif) is excused in small amounts (less than a quarter of the garment).
4
Note the inclusion of a child's urine
The passage mentions that 'urine' includes the urine of a child who has not yet started eating solid food. This is relevant for infant care.
The ruling on infant urine is also light impurity according to the school.
5
Apply the principle to gall and camel cud
The commentator extends the analogy: the gall (mararah) of any animal has the same ruling as its urine. The camel's cud (jurrah) is treated like its dung, as it originates from the stomach.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh uses analogy to cover similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراد بالبول كل بول سواء كان بول آدمي أو غيره إلا بول الخفاش فإنه طاهر كما سيأتي . وإلا بول ما يؤكل لحمه فإنه سيصرح بتخفيفه وأطلقه فشمل بول الصغير الذي لم يطعم، وشمل بول الهرة والفأرة وفيه اختلاف؛ ففي البزازية بول الهرة أو الفأرة إذا أصاب الثوب لا يفسده، وقيل إن زاد على قدر الدرهم أفسد وهو الظاهر وفي الخلاصة : إذا بالت الهرة في الإناء أو على الثوب تنجس، وكذا بول الفأرة. وقال الفقيه أو جعفر : ينجس الإناء دون الثوب ا هـ . وهو حسن لعادة تخمير الأواني. كذا في فتح القدير وفي المحيط : وخرء الفأرة وبولها نجس لأنه يستحيل إلى نتن وفساد والاحتراز عنه تمكن في الماء وغير ممكن في الطعام والثياب فصار معفواً فيهما ا هـ . وهو يفيد أن المراد بقول أبي جعفر ينجس الإناء أي إناء الماء لا مطلق الإناء، وفي فتاوى قاضيخان بول الهرة والفأرة وخرؤهما نجس في أظهر الروايات يفسد الماء والثوب، وبول الخفافيش وخرؤها لا يفسد لتعذر الاحتراز عنه ا هـ . وبهذا كله ظهر أن مراد صاحب التنجيس بنقل الاتفاق بقوله بال السنور في البئر نزح كله لأن بوله نجس باتفاق الروايات وكذا لو أصاب الثوب أفسده اتفاق الروايات الظاهرة لا مطلقاً الوجود الخلاف كما علمت وفي الظهيرية وبول الخفافيش ليس بنجس للضرورة، وكذلك بول الفأرة لأنه لا يمكن التحرز عنه ا هـ . وهو صريح في نفي النجاسة ثم قال آخراً: وبول الهرة نجس إلا على قول شاذ وفيها أيضاً ومرارة كل شيء كبوله، وجرة البعير حكمها حكم سرقينة لأنه توارى في جوفه والجرة بالكسر ما يخرجه البعير من جوفه إلى فمه فيأكله ثانياً. والسرقين الزبل.
And he intended by urine every urine, whether human or other, except the urine of bats for it is pure as will come. And except the urine of what is eaten for its meat, for he will explicitly say it is light. And he generalized it, so it includes the urine of a child who has not eaten food, and includes the urine of a cat and a mouse, and there is disagreement in it. In al-Bazzaziyyah: the urine of a cat or mouse if it hits the garment, it does not spoil it. And it is said that if it exceeds the size of a dirham, it spoils, and that is apparent. In al-Khulasa: if the cat urinates in the vessel or on the garment, it becomes impure, and also the urine of a mouse. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far said: it makes the vessel impure but not the garment. That is good due to the habit of covering vessels. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Muhit: the droppings of a mouse and its urine are impure because they turn to stench and corruption, and avoidance is possible in water but not possible in food and clothes, so it becomes excused in both. And it indicates that the meaning of Abu Ja'far's statement 'it makes the vessel impure' is the vessel of water, not the absolute vessel. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: the urine of cat and mouse and their droppings are impure in the most evident narrations; they spoil water and clothing. The urine of bats and their droppings do not spoil due to the impossibility of avoidance. Through all this, it becomes clear that the author of al-Tanjis meant by transmitting consensus saying 'the cat urinated in the well, draw all water' because its urine is impure by consensus of the narrations, and likewise if it hits the clothing, it spoils by consensus of the apparent narrations, not absolutely because the difference exists as you know. And in al-Zahiriyyah: the urine of bats is not impure due to necessity, and also the urine of the mouse because it is not possible to avoid it. That is explicit in negating impurity. Then he said lastly: the urine of the cat is impure except for an isolated opinion. And in it also: the gall of everything is like its urine, and the camel's cud has the same ruling as its dung because it is hidden in its stomach. And al-jurrah (with kasra) is what the camel brings out from its stomach to its mouth and eats it again. And al-sirqin is dung.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed discussion on the impurity of animal urine, highlighting intra-Hanafi disagreements. The matn stated that urine is generally impure, excluding bat urine (pure) and urine of edible animals (light impurity). The commentator expands on the urine of cats and mice, noting that there is ikhtilaf: some say it is impure only if it exceeds a dirham, others say it always spoils water and clothing. Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far makes a distinction between vessels (because vessels are often covered) and clothes. The principle of 'excused due to difficulty' (ma'fu) is applied to mouse urine because it is hard to avoid, especially in food and clothes. In water, it is not excused because avoidance is possible. The discussion ends with clarifying that the consensus narrated by the author of al-Tanjis is limited to apparent narrations, not absolute, because there is dissent. The bat urine is ruled not impure due to necessity. The passage also covers the ruling on gall (mararah) equating it to urine, and the camel's cud (jurrah) equated to dung.

Key Terms
تخفيف (takhfif): Light impurity, as opposed to مغلظ (heavy); for urine of edible animals.
معفو عنه (ma'fu 'anhu): Excused; the impurity is overlooked due to difficulty of avoidance.
تعذر الاحتراز (ta'adhur al-ihtiraz): Impossibility of avoidance; the reason for excusing bat and mouse urine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the urine of bats considered not impure (not najis)?
A) Because it is clean like water
B) Because it is impossible to avoid (ta'adhur al-ihtiraz)
C) Because it is light impurity
D) Because bats are not considered animals in fiqh

Key Takeaway
Urine is generally impure, but bat urine is pure due to necessity, and edible animal urine is light impurity.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Cat and Mouse Urine
Detailed intra-Hanafi dispute analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differing opinions among Hanafi scholars on cat and mouse urine, explain the principle of ta'adhur al-ihtiraz, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Cat urine is impure by consensus of apparent narrations; mouse urine is impure but excused in food and clothing.

The passage delves into the intra-Hanafi difference on the urine of cats and mice. While the generally transmitted position (zahir al-riwayah) holds that both are impure, some scholars like Abu Ja'far distinguish between vessels and garments, and others argue for excused impurity due to practical difficulty. This lesson unpacks the arguments and identifies the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main positions on cat urine
Some narrations say cat urine does not spoil a garment unless it exceeds a dirham; others say it always spoils. The passage notes that the 'most evident narrations' rule it impure (najis).
The position that it always spoils is the relied-upon view.
2
Analyze the distinction of Abu Ja'far
Al-Faqih Abu Ja'far differentiates: cat urine makes a vessel impure (because vessels are covered) but not a garment. This is considered 'hasan' (good) due to the custom of covering vessels.
This view is cited but not preferred.
3
Examine the treatment of mouse urine
According to al-Muhit, mouse urine is impure but becomes excused (ma'fu) in food and clothes because avoidance is difficult. In water, it is not excused because avoidance is possible.
This shows the application of ta'adhur al-ihtiraz.
4
Assess the consensus claim
The author of al-Tanjis claimed consensus that cat urine spoils water and garments. The commentator clarifies this is only the consensus of apparent narrations (zahir al-riwayah), as there is a dissenting view.
This nuance prevents overreaching the claim of consensus.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Zahiriyyah affirm that cat urine is impure, mouse urine is impure but excused where necessary, and bat urine is pure. This is the operative position.
Thus, the mufta bihi is consistent with the reasoning of difficulty.

Source Text

The Ruling of Martyr's Blood: Purity by Necessity
Expansion on the ruling of martyr's blood and its implications for prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on martyr's blood and explain the rationale of darura (necessity) that distinguishes its state when on the body versus when separated.

Core Idea
Martyr's blood is pure only due to necessity for prayer — once separated, the necessity ends and impurity returns.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the blood of a martyr. While blood is normally impure, the martyr's blood is an exception as long as it remains on his body, to permit his funeral prayer without washing. The commentary by Ibn Amir Haj and Rida al-Din explains the rationale of necessity (darura) and debates whether this dispensation extends to other dead bodies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states: 'As for the blood of the martyr, it is pure.' This is the starting point.
The ruling is not absolute; it requires conditions.
2
Understand the Condition
Ibn Amir Haj clarifies: the purity is conditional – only while the blood remains on the martyr's body. If it separates, it becomes impure.
The key is the presence of necessity (darura) for the funeral prayer.
3
Grasp the Rationale (Ta'lil)
The purity is due to necessity: performing the funeral prayer without washing is required, and the presence of blood is unavoidable. This necessity ceases when the blood separates.
Darura is a limited dispensation, not an intrinsic change in the blood's ruling.
4
Examine the Analogy
The commentator considers whether the same darura applies to a washed dead body with impurity. He concludes that the martyr's case is ittifaqi (agreed-upon unique exception) and should not be analogized.
This shows the discipline of restricting qiyas when the rationale is specific.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
If a garment is stained with separated martyr's blood exceeding a dirham, prayer is invalid due to impurity.
The dispensation is strictly for the martyr's funeral, not for subsequent contact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما دم الشهيد فهو طاهر الخ قال ابن أمير حاج : لأن دم الشهيد ما دام عليه محكوم بطهارته لضرورة جواز الصلاة عليه مع قيام الدم بخلاف ما لو انفصل الدم عنه فإنه يكون نجساً حتى لو أصاب ثوب إنسان أكبر من قدر الدرهم لم تجز صلاته لانعدام الضرورة حينئذ فلم يسقط اعتبار نجاسته ذكره رضى الدين في المحيط . ثم قال في أثناء المسألة التي بعدها قال العبد الضعيف غفر الله تعالى :له واعلم أن النظر إلى ما قدمناه عن المحيط من التعليل لجواز صلاة حامل الشهيد المتلطخ بدمائه الزائد على قدر الدرهم يفيد جواز صلاة حامل المسلم الميت المغسول الذي ليس بشهيد وقد أصابته نجاسة غليظة تزيد على قدر الدرهم لأن الظاهر أن النجاسة المذكورة به لا تمنع جواز الصلاة عليه، وحينئذ فوضع المسألة في الشهيد اتفاقي، وظاهر ما في مسألة الرضيع المذكورة يفيد عدم جواز صلاة حامل المسلم الميت المذكور وهو أوجه، وحينئذ فوضعه الشهيد غير اتفاقي ويحتاج إلى تعليل غير التعليل المذكور لها إلى آخر ما قال في الحلية فراجعه .
His statement: 'As for the blood of the martyr, it is pure, etc.' Ibn Amir Haj said: Because the blood of the martyr, as long as it remains on him, is ruled pure for the necessity of permitting prayer upon him while the blood is present, unlike if the blood separates from him, for then it becomes impure, so that if it hits a person's garment in an amount larger than a dirham, his prayer is not valid due to the lack of necessity then, so its impurity is not waived. Mentioned by Rida al-Din in al-Muhit. Then he said in the midst of the following issue: The weak servant, may Allah forgive him, said: Know that consideration of what we presented from al-Muhit regarding the rationale for the permissibility of the prayer of the carrier of the martyr smeared with his blood exceeding the size of a dirham indicates the permissibility of the prayer of the carrier of a washed dead Muslim who is not a martyr and has been hit by a heavy impurity exceeding the size of a dirham, because it appears that the mentioned impurity does not prevent the permissibility of prayer upon him. Then the placement of the issue in the martyr is agreed upon, and the apparent from the issue of the infant mentioned indicates the impermissibility of the prayer of the carrier of the said dead Muslim, and that is more appropriate. Then the placement of the martyr is not agreed upon and needs a rationale other than the mentioned one. [End quote from al-Hilya] So refer to it.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the earlier ruling regarding martyr's blood. Ibn Amir Haj explains that the martyr's blood is declared pure due to the necessity of performing his funeral prayer while the blood is still on his body. Once the blood separates from the body, the necessity ceases, and it becomes impure like other blood. If it contaminates clothing beyond a dirham's area, prayer becomes invalid. The author then quotes a discussion from al-Muhit by Rida al-Din, who draws an analogy: if the martyr's blood is excused due to necessity, then the same might apply to a washed dead body that has impurity. However, the issue is not settled, as the case of an infant (who died and was washed) is different. The author suggests that the martyr's case is unique (ittifaqi) and not analogized to other dead bodies. This shows the complexity of deriving rulings from necessity (darura).

Key Terms
ضرورة (darura): Necessity that exempts from normal impurity rulings.
اتفاقي (ittifaqi): Agreed upon; here it refers to the martyr's case being a unique, agreed-upon exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the ruling of martyr's blood when it separates from his body?
A) It remains pure
B) It becomes impure (najis)
C) It is excused (ma'fu) regardless of amount
D) It becomes mustahabb

Key Takeaway
Martyr's blood is pure only due to necessity for prayer; once it separates, normal impurity rules apply.

Heavy Impurities: Body Excretions and Intoxicating Drinks
Summary of heavy impurity classifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for heavy impurities from the human body and identify the preferred ruling for intoxicating drinks in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Anything exiting the body requiring wudu/ghusl is heavy impurity; all intoxicating drinks are also heavy impurity by preference.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a foundational legal maxim (qawida) that classifies impurities. It first establishes what substances from the human body are considered heavy impurities (najis ghaliz), then extends the ruling to all intoxicating drinks, resolving a known intra-Hanafi difference. Understanding this classification is essential for daily worship, as it determines the validity of prayer when such impurities are present.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the phrase 'كل ما يخرج من بدن الإنسان مما يوجب خروجه الوضوء أو الغسل فهو مغلظ'. This is the central maxim: anything that exits the body and requires wudu or ghusl is heavy impurity.
The rule links impurity (najasa) to ritual purification (hadath).
2
List the heavy impurities from the body
From the text, list the specific substances: feces, urine, semen, madhiy, wady, pus, serum, and vomit that fills the mouth. These are the examples given.
Lesser amounts of these (e.g., a small drop of urine) are not considered heavy; they may be light or excusable.
3
Understand the exception for other substances
The phrase 'أما ما دونه فطاهر' indicates that substances not requiring wudu/ghusl (like blood from a wound) are pure (tahir) on the correct view.
This is the default: purity unless proven otherwise.
4
Examine the ruling for intoxicating drinks
Wine (khamr) is heavy impurity by consensus due to its definitive prohibition. For other intoxicants (tila', sukr, naqi' al-zabib), there are three narrations in the Hanafi school: heavy, light, and pure.
The ikhtilaf arises because the prohibition of these drinks is based on zanni (speculative) evidence.
5
Apply the tarjih (preference)
The text states that the preferred view is to give weight to heaviness based on the general principle (الأصل المتقدم), making all intoxicating drinks heavy impurity like wine.
The definitive nature of wine's prohibition does not affect the illa: intoxication itself renders a substance impure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بالبول إلى أن كل ما يخرج من بدن الإنسان مما يوجب خروجه الوضوء أو الغسل فهو مغلظ كالغائط والبول والمني والمذي والودي والقيح والصديد والقيء إذا ملأ الفم، أما ما دونه فطاهر على الصحيح. وقيد بالخمر لأن بقية الأشربة المحرمه كالطلاء والسكر ونقيع الزبيب فيها ثلاث روايات : في رواية مغلظة، وفي أخرى مخففة، وفي أخرى طاهرة ذكرها في البدائع بخلاف الخمر فإنه مغلظ باتفاق الروايات لأن قطعية وحرمة غير الخمر ليست قطعية. وينبغي ترجيح التغليظ للأصل المتقدم كما لا يخفى فلا فرق بين الخمر وغيرها، وكون الحرمة فيه ليست قطعية لا التخفيف لأن دليل يوجب حرمتها
And he indicated by urine that everything that comes out of the human body whose exit necessitates wudu or ghusl is heavy impurity, like feces, urine, semen, madhiy, wady, pus, serum, and vomit if it fills the mouth. As for less than that, it is pure according to the correct view. And he restricted it to wine because the rest of the prohibited drinks like tila', sukr, and naqi' al-zabib have three narrations: in one narration heavy, in another light, in another pure, as mentioned in al-Bada'i. Unlike wine, it is heavy by consensus of narrations because its prohibition is definitive, and the prohibition of other than wine is not definitive. And it is preferable to give weight to heaviness due to the aforementioned principle, as is not hidden, so there is no difference between wine and others. The fact that the prohibition is not definitive does not necessitate lightness, because the evidence necessitates its prohibition.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage provides a general rule (qawida) for heavy impurities: anything that exits the human body and requires wudu or ghusl is heavy impurity (najis ghaliz). This includes feces, urine, semen, madhiy, wady, pus, serum, and vomit that fills the mouth. Smaller amounts of these are not considered impure. Then the text discusses intoxicating drinks: wine (khamr) is heavy impurity by consensus because its prohibition is definitive (qat'i). Other intoxicating drinks (like tila', sukr, naqi' al-zabib) have disputed rulings: some narrations classify them as heavy, others light, others pure. The preferred view (tarjih) is to classify them as heavy, based on the principle that heaviness is the default for impurities. The fact that their prohibition is not definitive (zanni) does not lighten the impurity ruling, because the effective cause (illa) is intoxication.

Key Terms
مغلظ (mghalliz): Heavy impurity; its presence in large amount invalidates prayer.
طلاء (tila'): A type of intoxicating drink made from grapes? This is a specific drink.
قطعية (qat'iyyah): Definitive; certainty of the prohibition (used for wine due to clear Quranic text).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
All intoxicating substances are heavy impurity, and any bodily excretion requiring ablution is heavy impurity.

Ruling on Urine and Droppings of Cat, Mouse, and Bat
Intra-Hanafi discussion with tarjih on animal urine and droppings impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the impurity of cat, mouse, and bat urine and droppings, and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Bat urine is not impure due to necessity; cat and mouse urine are impure, but there is difference on garments.

This passage dives into a nuanced intra-Hanafi debate about the impurity of certain animal excretions. While cat and mouse urine are generally considered impure, the text presents a disagreement over whether this impurity applies to garments in the same way as to vessels, and then introduces an exceptional ruling for bats based on necessity. Understanding this discussion sharpens your ability to reconcile multiple narrations and identify the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
State the default ruling for cat and mouse urine and droppings according to the majority of Hanafi sources.
Note that these are generally najis and spoil water and garments.
2
Note the ikhtilaf (Abu Ja'far)
Identify the dissenting view of al-Faqih Abu Ja'far: he distinguishes between a vessel (impure) and a garment (not impure).
The author calls this view 'good' due to custom of covering vessels.
3
Understand the reconciliation
Explain how later scholars interpret Abu Ja'far's 'vessel' to mean a water vessel, not any vessel, thus the difference is about possibility of avoidance.
This reconciliation shows the method of harmonizing conflicting narrations.
4
Identify the exceptional case (bat)
Recognize that the urine and droppings of bats are ruled not impure due to the difficulty of avoiding them.
This is confirmed as the fatwa by al-Haskafi and attributed to al-Tatarkhaniyyah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude which positions are relied upon for fatwa today.
Bat urine is not impure; cat and mouse urine are impure, but the earlier reported consensus on impurity is qualified as 'consensus of apparent narrations'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفسد، وقيل إن زاد على قدر الدرهم أفسد وهو الظاهر وفي الخلاصة : إذا بالت الهرة في الإناء أو على الثوب تنجس، وكذا بول الفأرة. وقال الفقيه أبو جعفر : ينجس الإناء دون الثوب ا هـ . وهو حسن لعادة تخمير الأواني. كذا في فتح القدير وفي المحيط : وخرء الفأرة وبولها نجس لأنه يستحيل إلى نتن وفساد والاحتراز عنه تمكن في الماء وغير ممكن في الطعام والثياب فصار معفواً فيهما ا هـ . وهو يفيد أن المراد بقول أبي جعفر ينجس الإناء أي إناء الماء لا مطلق الإناء، وفي فتاوى قاضيخان بول الهرة والفأرة وخرؤهما نجس في أظهر الروايات يفسد الماء والثوب، وبول الخفافيش وخرؤها لا يفسد لتعذر الاحتراز عنه ا هـ . وبهذا كله ظهر أن مراد صاحب التنجيس بنقل الاتفاق بقوله بال السنور في البئر نزح كله لأن بوله نجس باتفاق الروايات وكذا لو أصاب الثوب أفسده اتفاق الروايات الظاهرة لا مطلقاً الوجود الخلاف كما علمت وفي الظهيرية وبول الخفافيش ليس بنجس للضرورة (الخ قال الشيخ علاء الدين الحصكفي : وعليه الفتوى وعزاه إلى التتارخانية . الخلاصة من
It spoils, and it is said that if it exceeds the size of a dirham it spoils, and that is apparent. In al-Khulasah: If a cat urinates in a vessel or on a garment, it becomes impure, and similarly the urine of a mouse. Al-Faqih Abu Ja’far said: It makes the vessel impure but not the garment. This is good due to the custom of covering vessels. Similar is in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Muhit: The droppings of a mouse and its urine are impure because it transforms into stench and decay, and avoidance of it is possible in water but not possible in food and clothes, so it is pardoned in both. This indicates that Abu Ja’far’s statement that it makes the vessel impure means the vessel of water, not any vessel. In Fatawa Qadikhan: The urine and droppings of a cat and mouse are impure in the most apparent narrations, spoiling water and garments. The urine and droppings of bats do not spoil due to the difficulty of avoiding them. By all this it becomes clear that the intention of the author of al-Tanjis in reporting consensus by his words: ‘If a cat urinates in a well, all its water is removed because its urine is impure by consensus of narrations, and similarly if it hits a garment it spoils it by consensus of the apparent narrations, not absolutely, as the difference exists as you learned. In al-Zahiriyyah: The urine of bats is not impure due to necessity. Al-Shaykh Ala’ al-Din al-Haskafi said: And upon it is the fatwa, and he attributed it to al-Tatarkhaniyyah. Summary from.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi discussion on the impurity of urine and droppings of cats, mice, and bats. The author collects several narrations and opinions, reconciling them. The base ruling is that cat and mouse urine is impure (najis) and spoils water and garments beyond a dirham size. Abu Ja’far distinguishes between a vessel (which becomes impure) and a garment (which does not), but this is explained as referring to a water vessel where avoidance is easier. Bat urine and droppings are ruled not impure due to the difficulty of avoiding them, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. The passage ends by confirming that the earlier report of consensus on cat urine refers to the apparent narrations, acknowledging the existence of other views.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the impurity of animal excretions is determined by the possibility of avoidance; necessity and custom can lead to exceptions like bat urine.

Classification of Thick Impurities from the Human Body
Definition of thick impurities from human body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for classifying bodily substances as thick impurities and identify which listed substances meet that criterion.

Core Idea
Any substance whose exit requires wudu or ghusl is a thick impurity; lesser emissions are pure.

In the Hanafi school, impurities are graded into thick (ghalīẓah) and light (khafīfah). This lesson presents a clear, memorable criterion: if the exit of a substance from the body necessitates wudu or ghusl, it is a thick impurity. Mastering this principle allows instant classification of urine, semen, pus, and more, enabling correct rulings on purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Criterion
Locate the condition: 'the exiting of which necessitates wudu or ghusl'. This is the legal cause (ʿillah) for thick impurity classification.
The criterion is functional, not based on origin or dirtiness.
2
Apply to the Listed Substances
Apply the criterion to each substance: feces, urine, semen, madhy, wady, pus, ichor, and mouth-filling vomit all meet it, so they are thick impurities.
Vomit is only thick if it fills the mouth; less is pure.
3
Recognize the Exception
Substances not requiring wudu or ghusl (e.g., sweat, tears, saliva) are pure (ṭāhir) according to the correct view.
This prevents over-extension of impurity rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بالبول إلى أن كل ما يخرج من بدن الإنسان مما يوجب خروجه الوضوء أو الغسل فهو مغلظ كالغائط والبول والمني والمذي والودي والقيح والصديد والقيء إذا ملأ الفم، أما ما دونه فطاهر على الصحيح.
He pointed out by 'urine' that everything that exits from the human body, the exiting of which necessitates wudu or ghusl, is thick (impurity), like feces, urine, semen, madhy, wady, pus, ichor, and vomit if it fills the mouth. As for less than that, it is pure according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This is a clear ruling statement defining which bodily substances are considered thick impurities (najāsat ghalīẓah). The criterion is whether their exit requires wudu or ghusl. If the substance necessitates ablution (wudu) — such as urine, feces, madhy, wady, pus, ichor, and vomit that fills the mouth — then it is a thick impurity. If the substance does not require wudu or ghusl (e.g., sweat, tears), then it is pure (tāhir). The phrase 'on the correct view' indicates there is a difference of opinion but this is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
مغلظ (mghallaẓ): Thick impurity (najāsat ghalīẓah) requiring complete removal if above the exempted amount.
الوضوء (al-wuḍū'): Ablution, ritual washing before prayer.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Full body wash required after major impurities.
المذي (al-madhī): Pre-seminal fluid, requires wudu.
الودي (al-wadī): Post-urine discharge, requires wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because its exit requires ghusl.
C) Because it is dirty.
D) Because it is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of thick impurities is based solely on whether the substance's exit necessitates wudu or ghusl.

6 min
Ruling on Wine vs Other Intoxicating Drinks as Impurities
Tarjih among three narrations on impurity of non-wine intoxicants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preponderant Hanafi view on the impurity of non-wine intoxicating drinks and explain the basis for tarjih among three narrations.

Core Idea
All intoxicating drinks are thick impurities – the preponderant view unifies the ruling despite differences in certitude.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, the author addresses the ruling on intoxicating drinks other than wine. While wine is unanimously considered a thick impurity (najāsah ghalīẓah) because its prohibition is definitive, other intoxicants like ṭilā’, sakar, and raisin soak have three narrations. The author performs tarjīḥ (preponderance) to establish that they too are thick impurities, based on the general principle that prohibition due to intoxication implies impurity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequence for prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three narrations
List the three positions on non-wine intoxicants: thick (mughallazah), light (mukhaffafah), and pure (tāhirah). Note that these are mentioned in al-Badā’i‘.
2
Distinguish wine from other intoxicants
Wine is thick by consensus because its prohibition is qaṭ‘ī (definitive). Other intoxicants' prohibition is ẓannī (probable), leading to ikhtilāf.
The author uses this distinction to explain why other intoxicants have multiple narrations.
3
Evaluate the tarjīḥ argument
The author prefers thickness based on the principle (aṣl) that prohibition of intoxication entails impurity. The lack of definitive proof does not weaken this; even a probable prohibition demands avoidance.
The key point: 'كون الحرمة فيه ليست قطیة لا التخفیف' – the absence of certainty does not imply lightness.
4
Apply the practical consequence
If such liquid touches a garment above a dirham, prayer is invalid. This confirms the thick impurity ruling.
This shows the direct effect on 'ibādah.
5
Identify the supporting authority
Raḍī al-Dīn in al-Muḥīṭ supports this tarjīḥ, strengthening its reliability.
Recognize the chain of authority: later scholars upheld this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالخمر لأن بقية الأشربة المحرمه كالطلاء والسكر ونقيع الزبيب فيها ثلاث روايات : في رواية مغلظة، وفي أخرى مخففة، وفي أخرى طاهرة ذكرها في البدائع بخلاف الخمر فإنه مغلظ باتفاق الروايات لأن قطعية وحرمة غير الخمر ليست قطعية. وينبغي ترجيح التغليظ للأصل المتقدم كما لا يخفى فلا فرق بين الخمر وغيرها، وكون الحرمة فيه ليست قطعية لا التخفيف لأن دليل يوجب حرمتها يكون نجسا حتى لو أصاب ثوب إنسان أكبر من قدر الدرهم لم تجز صلاته لانعدام الضرورة حينئذ فلم يسقط اعتبار نجاسته ذكره رضى الدين في المحيط .
He restricted it to wine because regarding the rest of the prohibited drinks — like ṭilā’, sakar, and naqī‘ al-zabīb — there are three narrations: in one narration [they are] thick, in another light, and in another pure, as mentioned in al-Badā’i‘. Unlike wine, which is thick by consensus of narrations, because its prohibition is definitive, and the prohibition of other than wine is not definitive. It is preferable to give preponderance to thickness based on the aforementioned principle, as is not hidden, so there is no difference between wine and others. And the fact that the prohibition of them is not definitive does not [imply] lightness, because evidence that necessitates their prohibition makes them impure, such that if it hits a person’s garment exceeding the size of a dirham, his prayer is not valid due to the absence of necessity then, so consideration of its impurity is not dropped. This was mentioned by Raḍī al-Dīn in al-Muḥīṭ.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the impurity of intoxicating drinks other than wine. The author notes three narrations in the Hanafi school regarding non-wine intoxicants (like ṭilā’ (boiled grape juice), sakar (date wine), and raisin soak). Some say they are thick impurities, some light, some pure. For wine itself, there is consensus it is thick, because its prohibition is established by definitive evidence (Qur’an and mutawatir sunnah). However, the author argues that the correct view (tarjīḥ) is to consider all intoxicating drinks as thick impurities, because the general principle (aṣl) is that anything prohibited due to intoxication is impure, and there is no strong evidence to differentiate. The argument that their prohibition is only likely (ẓannī) does not negate impurity, as even a likely prohibition still demands avoiding the substance. Therefore, if such a liquid touches a garment beyond a dirham, prayer is invalid. This is the position of Raḍī al-Dīn al-Sarakhsī in al-Muḥīṭ.

Key Terms
الطلاء (al-ṭilā'): Boiled grape juice that is intoxicating
السكر (al-sakar): Date wine or any intoxicating drink from dates
نقيع الزبيب (naqī‘ al-zabīb): Raisin soak that becomes intoxicating
قطعية (qaṭ‘iyyah): Definitive (in proof)

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They are light impurities (najāsah khafīfah).
C) They are thick impurities (najāsah ghalīẓah).
D) They are impure only if used to intoxicate.

Key Takeaway
All intoxicating drinks are thick impurities in the preponderant Hanafi view; the lack of definitive prohibition does not diminish impurity status.

Carrying a Non-Martyr Deceased with Blood Impurity
Analogical reasoning (qiyas) on impurity of blood on the deceased

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi qiyas to distinguish between the ruling for carrying a martyr and a non-martyr deceased with thick impurity, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The impurity on a martyr is excused; on a non-martyr it invalidates the carrier's prayer.

This passage explores a nuanced point in Hanafi taharah: whether the concession granted for carrying a martyr smeared with blood extends to a non-martyr who has been washed but still bears impurity. The author presents two rationales—one generalising the concession, the other restricting it. The second, derived from the case of an infant, is deemed stronger (awjah). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the validity of prayer while carrying a deceased person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling context
The act under discussion is carrying a deceased person during prayer when the deceased has a thick impurity (e.g., blood) exceeding a dirham. Determine the basic Hanafi rule: carrying such impurity normally invalidates prayer (batil).
The dirham-sized exception is standard for najasah ghalizah.
2
Recognise the martyr's exception
For a martyr (al-shahid), the blood on his body is considered pure in some respects, so carrying him even with blood exceeding a dirham does not invalidate the prayer. This is established in al-Muhit.
Martyr is not washed before burial.
3
Understand the qiyas to a non-martyr
The passage extends the rationale (ta'lil) from the martyr to the non-martyr: if the impurity is attached to the deceased's body, perhaps it is excused. But this analogy is questioned because the non-martyr has been washed, so the remaining impurity is separate.
The 'infant issue' (رضيع) provides a parallel where carrying a non-martyr with impurity is not allowed.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states that the apparent meaning from the infant issue indicates impermissibility, and that is 'awjah' (more appropriate). Thus the stronger position is that carrying a non-martyr with thick impurity invalidates the carrier's prayer.
The author refers to al-Hilyah for further detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال في أثناء المسألة التي بعدها قال العبد الضعيف غفر الله تعالى :له واعلم أن النظر إلى ما قدمناه عن المحيط من التعليل لجواز صلاة حامل الشهيد المتلطخ بدمائه الزائد على قدر الدرهم يفيد جواز صلاة حامل المسلم الميت المغسول الذي ليس بشهيد وقد أصابته نجاسة غليظة تزيد على قدر الدرهم لأن الظاهر أن النجاسة المذكورة به لا تمنع جواز الصلاة عليه، وحينئذ فوضع المسألة في الشهيد اتفاقي، وظاهر ما في مسألة الرضيع المذكورة يفيد عدم جواز صلاة حامل المسلم الميت المذكور وهو أوجه، وحينئذ فوضعه الشهيد غير اتفاقي ويحتاج إلى تعليل غير التعليل المذكور لها إلى آخر ما قال في الحلية فراجعه .
Then he said in the course of the issue after it, the humble servant, may Allah forgive him, said: ‘Know that consideration of what we have presented from al-Muḥīṭ regarding the rationale for the permissibility of the prayer of the carrier of a martyr smeared with his blood exceeding the size of a dirham indicates the permissibility of the prayer of the carrier of a deceased Muslim who has been washed and is not a martyr, and a thick impurity more than a dirham has touched him, because evidently the mentioned impurity does not prevent the permissibility of prayer upon him. Therefore, the placement of the issue [in the context of] the martyr is incidental, and the apparent [meaning] in the issue of the infant mentioned indicates the impermissibility of the prayer of the carrier of the said deceased Muslim, and that is more appropriate. Therefore, the placement of the martyr is not incidental and requires a rationale other than the mentioned rationale, up to the end of what he said in al-Ḥilyah. So refer to it.’
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is an analytical discussion (from the commentary of ‘Abd al-Ḍa‘īf) on whether carrying a deceased person with blood impurity exceeding a dirham invalidates the prayer of the carrier. The ruling for a martyr is that it is permitted. The question is whether this extends to a non-martyr deceased who has been washed but still has some impurity. The author notes that the rationale given in al-Muḥīṭ for the martyr (that the impurity is attached to the body of the deceased and is excused) would logically apply to any deceased Muslim. However, another opinion (from the issue of an infant) suggests it is not permitted for a non-martyr, which is considered stronger (awjah). Thus, the ruling for the martyr is not simply incidental but has a specific rationale. The passage ends by referring the reader to al-Ḥilyah for more details.

Key Terms
الشهيد (al-shahīd): Martyr who dies in the battlefield without being washed
الرضيع (al-raḍī‘): Infant, mentioned in a related issue for analogy
أوجه (awjah): More appropriate; stronger (in terms of tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which view is considered 'awjah' (stronger) regarding carrying a non-martyr deceased with thick impurity exceeding a dirham?
A) The prayer is valid because the impurity is on the deceased.
B) The prayer is invalid because the non-martyr has been washed.
C) The prayer is valid only if the impurity is less than a dirham.
D) The prayer is invalid only if the impurity is from blood.

Key Takeaway
The concession for carrying a martyr with blood impurity does not extend to a non-martyr who has been washed; the latter invalidates the carrier's prayer.

Defining 'Definitive Proof' for Thick Impurities: A Hanafi Refinement
Critical refinement of the definition of thick impurities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of 'dalīl qaṭ‘ī' in the context of thick impurities, and distinguish it from common misunderstandings regarding transmission certainty.

Core Idea
Thick impurities are established by evidence that is definitive in action, not necessarily definitive in transmission.

In al‑Hidāyah, the classification of thick impurities is tied to the concept of 'definitive proof'. Yet later commentators refined this: it does not mean the proof must be transmitted with absolute certainty (qaṭ‘ī al‑thubūt), but rather that the proof is definitive in obligating action (qaṭ‘ī fī al‑‘amal). In this lesson, we will unpack this crucial distinction and see how it preserves the validity of relying on probable evidence (ẓannī) for practical rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim in al‑Hidāyah
The author of al‑Hidāyah states that thick impurities are established by a definitive proof. Locate the exact phrase in the passage: 'لأنها ثبتت بدلیل مقطوع به'.
This claim is the starting point for the refinement.
2
Examine the commentary in Fatḥ al‑Qadīr
Ibn al‑Humām interprets 'definitive proof' as مقطوع بوجوب العمل به (definitive in requiring action). This shifts the emphasis from transmission to obligation.
Even ẓannī evidence must be acted upon definitively in furū‘ matters.
3
Analyze the alternative explanation in al‑‘Ināyah
Al‑‘Ināyah adds that 'definitive proof' means the evidence is free from factors that would lighten the ruling, such as conflicting texts, contradictory ijtihād, or overriding necessities.
This ensures the severity of thick impurities is maintained.
4
Synthesize the refined definition
Combine both commentaries: dalīl qaṭ‘ī here means evidence that is both binding in action and unmitigated by any countervailing factors.
This is a specific technical meaning, not the general usūlī sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التغليظ لا يشترط أن يكون قطعياً. وأما قول صاحب الهداية بعد ذكر النجاسات الغليظة لأنها ثبتت بدلیل مقطوع به فقال في فتح القدير : معناه مقطوع بوجوب العمل به فالعمل بالظني واجب قطعاً في الفروع وإن كان نفس وجوب مقتضاه ظنياً والأولى أن يريد دليل الإجماع ا هـ . وفي العناية : المراد بالدليل القطعي أن يكون سالماً من الأسباب الموجبة للتخفيف من تعارض النصين وتجاذب الاجتهاد والضرورات المخففة ا هـ .
Thickness does not require that [the proof] be definitive. As for the statement of the author of al-Hidayah after mentioning the thick impurities — 'because they are established by a definitive proof' — he said in Fatḥ al-Qadīr: 'It means definitive in requiring action upon it, so acting upon ẓannī (probable evidence) is definitively obligatory in branch matters, even if the obligation of its implication is ẓannī. It is better that he intends the proof of consensus.' In al-‘Ināyah: 'The meaning of definitive proof is that it be free from causes that necessitate lightness, such as contradiction of two texts, conflicting ijtihad, and lightening necessities.'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of what is meant by 'definitive proof' (dalīl qaṭ‘ī) in the context of thick impurities. The author of al-Hidayah claimed that thick impurities are established by definitive proof. The commentators explain that this does not mean the proof must be qaṭ‘ī al-thubūt (definitively transmitted), but rather qaṭ‘ī fī al-‘amal (definitive in requiring action). Even if the evidence is ẓannī (likely), acting upon it is obligatory in practical matters. Al-‘Ināyah adds that 'definitive proof' here means free from factors that would lighten the ruling, such as conflicting texts, divergent ijtihad, or necessities that ease the ruling. Thus, the categorization of thick impurities depends on the strength and clarity of the evidence, not absolute certainty in transmission.

Key Terms
الدليل القطعي (al-dalīl al-qaṭ‘ī): Evidence that definitively requires action, either due to certainty in transmission or clarity of meaning, or due to absence of mitigating factors

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'definitive proof' (dalīl qaṭ‘ī) mean in the context of thick impurities, according to Fatḥ al-Qadīr?
A) The proof must be from the Quran.
B) The proof must be mutawatir hadith.
C) The proof is definitive in requiring action, even if its transmission is probable.
D) The proof must be agreed upon by all scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of thick impurities relies on evidence that is definitive in obligating action and free from countervailing factors, not solely on transmission certainty.

Ruling on Bird Droppings: Pure, Light, or Thick Impurity
Ruling expansion classifying bird droppings by flight and edibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify bird droppings as pure, light impurity, or thick impurity by applying the Hanafi criteria of flight and edibility, and state the relied-upon position for chicken, duck, and goose.

Core Idea
Bird droppings are judged by flight habit and edibility: ground birds = thick, flying edible = pure, flying inedible = light.

Not all bird droppings are treated equally under Hanafi fiqh. The ruling depends on whether the bird defecates while flying (yadhriq) and whether its meat is eaten. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq expands on the classification, showing how custom and difficulty of avoidance shape the ruling. We will trace the criteria and apply them to common birds like chicken, duck, goose, pigeon, and hawk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key criteria
The passage uses two criteria: (1) whether the bird defecates in the air while flying, and (2) whether its meat is eaten. These determine the category of impurity.
The term 'yadhriq' means to defecate during flight.
2
Classify non-flying ground birds
Birds that do not fly (or fly rarely and live among people) – like chicken, duck, and goose in one narration – have droppings that are thick impurity (najāsah ghalīẓah) because they are repulsive (mustaqdhar) and resemble human feces.
This is the default for barnyard fowl.
3
Classify flying edible birds
Birds that fly and defecate in the air and whose meat is eaten – like pigeon and sparrow – have pure droppings. They are not considered impure because avoiding them would be difficult.
This ruling appears in the section on wells.
4
Classify flying inedible birds
Birds that fly but whose meat is not eaten – like falcon, hawk, kite – have light impurity (najāsah khafīfah). They are impure but less severely, with different rules for purification.
The text promises further discussion on this difference.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on goose
For goose, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one considers droppings pure, the other thick impurity. The text does not give a final preference, so the mufti may choose.
Abu Yusuf narrated pure; al-Hasan narrated thick.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بخرء الدجاج إلى خرء كل طير لا يذرق في الهواء كالدجاج والبط لوجود معنى النجاسة فيه وهو كونه مستقذراً لتغييره إلى نتن وفساد رائحة فأشبه العذرة. وفي الاوز عن أبي حنيفة روايتان، روى أبو يوسف عنه أنه ليس بنجس، وروى الحسن عنه أنه نجس كذا في البدائع. وفي البزازية : وخرء البط إذا كان يعيش بين الناس ولا يطير فكالدجاج، وإن كان يطير ولا يعيش بين الناس فكالحمامة، وقيد به لأن خرء الطيور التي تذرق في الهواء نوعان: فما يؤكل لحمه كالحمام والعصفور فقد تقدم في بحث الآبار أنه طاهر، وما لا يؤكل لحمه كالصقر والبازي والحدأة فسيذكر أنه مخفف وفيه خلاف نبينه إن شاء الله تعالى.
He pointed out by 'chicken droppings' to the droppings of every bird that does not defecate in the air, like chicken and duck, because the meaning of impurity is found in it, namely being repulsive due to its change into stench and putrefaction of smell, so it resembles human feces. Regarding goose, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf narrated from him that it is not impure, and al-Hasan narrated from him that it is impure, as in al-Badā’i‘. In al-Bazzāziyyah: The droppings of a duck, if it lives among people and does not fly, are like chicken; if it flies and does not live among people, it is like a pigeon. This restriction because the droppings of birds that defecate in the air are of two types: what is eaten (like pigeon and sparrow) has already been discussed in the section on wells that it is pure; what is not eaten (like falcon, hawk, kite) will be mentioned as light, and there is a difference of opinion which we will explain, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents rulings on bird droppings based on the bird's behavior (whether it flies and defecates in the air) and edibility. The key distinction: birds that do not fly and live on the ground (chicken, duck, goose in one narration) have droppings that are thick impurities (najāsah ghalīẓah) because they are repulsive and similar to human waste. For goose, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one says it is not impure (pure), the other says it is impure. For duck, it depends on whether it flies and lives with people. Birds that fly and defecate in the air are further divided: if their meat is eaten (like pigeon, sparrow), their droppings are pure; if their meat is not eaten (like falcon, hawk), their droppings are light impurities (najāsah khafīfah). This nuanced classification shows the Hanafi method of balancing textual evidence with practical custom and difficulty of avoidance.

Key Terms
يذرق (yadhriq): Defecates in the air (while flying)
مستقذر (mustaqdhar): Repulsive, considered filthy by custom
مخفف (mukhaffaf): Light impurity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the droppings of a pigeon that lives among people and flies?
A) Thick impurity.
B) Light impurity.
C) Pure.
D) Impure only if it exceeds a dirham.

Key Takeaway
Bird droppings are classified based on flight and edibility: ground birds = thick impurity, flying edible = pure, flying inedible = light impurity.

The Impurity of Animal Dung: Hanafi Positions vs. Malik
Intra-Hanafi dispute with Malik on animal dung impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) on the impurity of animal dung, state the evidence (dalil) for each view, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for prayer validity.

Core Idea
The hadith 'It is riks' makes dung a thick impurity; affliction (balwā) affects shoes but not the general ruling.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school and with Imam Malik regarding the impurity status of animal dung from donkeys, horses, cows, and camels. Abu Hanifa considers it a thick impurity (najāsah ghalīẓah) based on a prophetic hadith, while his students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) classify it as light, and Imam Malik deems it pure. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies how the Hanafi school balances textual evidence with practical necessity (balwā).

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter
The passage discusses the impurity of dung from specific animals: rawth (donkey/horse), khathi (cow), and ba'r (camel). First, note the agreed-upon cases (human feces, dog saliva, predator regurgitation) before focusing on the disputed ones.
The key terms are defined in the passage: al-rawth, al-khathi, al-ba'r.
2
State Abu Hanifa's view and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that these dungs are thick impurities (najāsah ghalīẓah). His primary evidence is the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) said concerning dung, 'It is riks'—meaning impure. In Hanafi usul, the word 'riks' indicates impurity and, by extension, thickness.
The hadith is referenced as 'innaha riks' (إنها ركس).
3
State the Sahibayn's view and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad classify the same dungs as light impurities (najāsah khafīfah). Their proof is the principle of 'public affliction' (balwā) because roads and markets are filled with dung, making strict avoidance impractical. They also note that Imam Malik goes further, considering it pure.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (tarjih)
The passage defends Abu Hanifa by asserting that the text (the hadith) cannot be overridden by mere affliction. Affliction is already accounted for in the lenient ruling for sandals (purified by rubbing). Extending that leniency to other cases lacks justification. The debate is resolved in favor of Abu Hanifa's view in the relied-upon (muftā bihī) position.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: If dung contacts the body or clothing, under Abu Hanifa, if it exceeds a dirham in area or amount, prayer is invalid unless fully removed. Under the Sahibayn, since it is light, the dirham exemption applies. Under Malik, no action is needed as it is pure.
This difference directly affects the validity of salah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بالروث والخثي إلى نجاسة خرء كل حيوان غير الطيور، فالروث للحمار والفرس والخثي للبقر، والبعر للإبل، والغائط للآدمي، ولا خلاف في تغليظ غائط الآدمي ونجو الكلب ورجيع السباع واختلفوا فيما عداه؛ فعنده غليظة لقوله عليه السلام في الروثة إنها ركس» أي نجس ولم يعارض، وعندهما خفيفة فإن مالكاً يرى طهارتها ولعموم البلوى لامتلاء الطرق بخلاف بول الحمار وغيره مما لا يؤكل لحمه لأن الأرض تنشفه حتى رجع محمد آخر إلى أنه لا يمنع الروث وإن فحش لما دخل الري مع الخليفة ورأى بلوى الناس من امتلاء الطرق والخانات بها. وقاس المشايخ على قوله هذا طين بخارى لأن مشي الناس والدواب فيها واحد، وعند . ذلك رجوعه في الخف حتى إذا أصابته عذرة يطهر بالدلك وفي الروث لا يحتاج إلى الدلك عنده. ولأبي حنيفة أن الموجب للعمل النص لا الخلاف والبلوى في النعال وقد ظهر أثرها حتى طهرت بالدلك فإثبات أمر زائد على ذلك يكون بغير موجب، وما قيل إن البلوى لا تعتبر في موضع النص عنده كبول الإنسان فممنوع، بل تعتبر إذا تحققت بالنص النافي للحرج وهو ليس معارضة للنص بالرأي. كذا في فتح القدير .
He pointed out by 'al-rawth and al-khathī' to the impurity of the droppings of every animal other than birds. Al-rawth is for donkey and horse, al-khathī for cow, al-ba‘r for camel, and al-ghā’iṭ for humans. There is no disagreement about the thickness of human feces, dog saliva, and the regurgitated matter of predators. They differed regarding what else [that is mentioned]. According to him (Abu Hanifa), it is thick, based on his statement (peace be upon him) concerning dung: 'It is riks', i.e., impure, and it is not contradicted. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is light; indeed Malik views it as pure, due to the prevalence of affliction (general need) because the roads are filled [with dung], unlike the urine of a donkey or other non-edible animals, because the earth absorbs it. Muhammad later retracted, saying that dung does not prevent [prayer] even if excessive, because when he entered Ray with the caliph and saw the difficulty people faced from the roads and inns being filled with it. The shaykhs analogized to his statement this: 'The clay of Bukhara [is like that]' because the walking of people and animals is one. At that point, he retracted regarding [impurity] in shoes, so that if feces touches them, they are purified by rubbing (dalk), and for dung, he does not require rubbing. According to Abu Hanifa, what necessitates action is the text, not disagreement or affliction; and affliction regarding sandals is [already] evident, so they are purified by dalk. Establishing an additional [ruling] beyond that is without cause. And as for what is said that affliction is not considered in the presence of a text, like human urine, is rejected. Rather, it is considered if it is realized by a text negating hardship, and that is not opposing the text with opinion. Thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a detailed inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute about the impurity of animal dung (from donkeys, horses, cows, camels). Abu Hanifa holds it is a thick impurity (najāsah ghalīẓah), based on the hadith where the Prophet called dung 'riks' (impure). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Ṣāḥibayn) consider it a light impurity, emphasizing widespread need (balwā) because roads and markets are full of dung, making avoidance difficult. Imam Malik goes further, deeming it pure. Muhammad later retracted his lighter view after seeing the difficulty in Ray, ruling that dung (even if excessive) does not prevent prayer, and that shoes soiled by dung do not require washing but only rubbing (dalk). The passage concludes with a defense of Abu Hanifa's position from Fatḥ al-Qadīr: the text (hadith) is the operative basis, not the disagreement or affliction. The affliction already recognized in sandals (which are purified by dalk) does not justify extending the same leniency to garments or other situations. The final view of the text sides with Abu Hanifa, emphasizing the hadith as primary evidence.

Key Terms
الروث (al-rawth): Dung of donkey or horse
الخثي (al-khathī): Cow dung (rarely used; usually 'khuthba')
البعر (al-ba‘r): Camel dung (pellet-like)
الرجيع (al-rajī‘): Regurgitated matter of predators
الدلك (al-dalk): Rubbing to purify (for impurities that can be removed by rubbing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence Abu Hanifa uses to rule that animal dung is a thick impurity?
A) Consensus of the Companions.
B) The hadith that dung is 'riks'.
C) The widespread affliction on roads.
D) The analogy to human feces.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, animal dung is classified as a thick impurity based on the hadith, but a leniency (rubbing) is granted for sandals due to public necessity.

Ruling on Grain in Animal Dung and Bread with Mouse Droppings
Practical rulings on impurities in food

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on impurities in food by distinguishing between solid and crumbly textures and between dry and soft animal dung.

Core Idea
Impurity in food is judged by texture: solid prevents spread, crumbly allows penetration.

This passage from Baḥr al-Rāʾiq presents two specific cases where food items are found mixed with impurities. The Hanafi school carefully considers the physical properties of the substances involved—especially solidity and dryness—to determine whether the food can be salvaged. Understanding these rulings reveals a deeper principle: impurity spread depends on the medium's texture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two separate scenarios: (1) barley found inside camel or sheep dung, and (2) bread in which mouse droppings are found. Note that each has its own conditions.
2
Analyze the condition for grain in dung
For barley in camel or sheep dung, the ruling is to wash and eat it. Why? Because these dungs are dry and solid—impurity does not penetrate the grain. In contrast, cow dung lacks solidity, so the impurity spreads into the grain, making it unsalvageable.
Distinction based on the dung's solidity (ṣalābah).
3
Analyze the condition for bread with mouse droppings
If bread is solid (ṣalīb), only the part with the dropping is removed, and the rest is eaten. The text cuts off for crumbly (mutafattit) bread, implying that impurity may have spread throughout, requiring a different ruling—likely the entire bread is impure unless washed (if possible).
The completion of the ruling is found in Mukhtārāt al-Nawāzil.
4
Derive the general principle
The common factor is whether the impurity has penetrated the food. Dryness and solidity act as barriers; softness and crumbliness allow penetration. This is a case of applying the maxim 'al-yaqīn lā yazūlu bi-l-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt) coupled with assessment of probable penetration.
For fatwa, the mufti must determine texture on a case-by-case basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية والشعير الذي يوجد في بعر الإبل والشاة يغسل ويؤكل بخلاف ما يوجد في خثي البقر لأنه لا صلابة فيه. خبز وجد في خلاله خرء الفأرة فإن كان صلباً يرمى قوله : (فإن كان صلباً الخ قال ابن أمير :حاج زاد في مختارات النوازل وإن كان متفتتاً ما لم
In al-Ẓahīriyyah: Barley that is found in camel dung and sheep dung is washed and eaten, unlike what is found in cow dung because there is no solidity in it. Bread in the midst of which mouse droppings are found: if it is solid, [the affected part] is thrown away. His statement: 'If it is solid...' Ibn Amīr Ḥāj added in Mukhtārāt al-Nawāzil: 'And if it is crumbly, as long as it...'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents two practical rulings regarding impurities mixed with food. First: if barley (grain) is found in camel or sheep dung, it can be washed and eaten because it is not spoiled. But if found in cow dung (which is softer and more penetrating), it is not to be eaten. Second: if bread has mouse droppings inside, the ruling depends on the bread's solidity. If solid, the area with the dropping is removed and the rest eaten. If the bread is crumbly (and the impurity may have spread), the text cuts off but implies further ruling. The citation from Mukhtārāt al-Nawāzil by Ibn Amīr Ḥāj likely says that if crumbly, the entire bread is to be avoided unless it can be washed. This shows careful consideration of how impurity spreads based on texture.

Key Terms
صلابة (ṣalābah): Solidity, firmness; here referring to the texture of bread that prevents impurity from spreading
متفتت (mutafattit): Crumbly, easy to break; here implying impurity may have penetrated

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what should be done with barley found in camel dung?
A) It is thrown away.
B) It is washed and eaten.
C) It is fed to animals.
D) It is used as fertilizer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the consumption of food mixed with dry animal dung after washing, but prohibits it if the dung is soft or the food is crumbly, due to impurity penetration.

Dispensations for Impurities from Camel Dung, Mud, and Stable Manure
States rulings on common impurity dispensations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on prayer validity when common impurities such as camel dung, mud, or stable manure are present, and identify the conditions under which a dispensation applies.

Core Idea
Small, non-visible impurities from common sources do not invalidate prayer due to custom and necessity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses three practical scenarios where impurities are commonly encountered: camel dung falling into milk during milking, mud on clothes from walking, and stable manure on feet. The Hanafi school grants dispensations in these cases, balancing legal purity (taharah) with the realities of daily life. The author also reveals an intra-Hanafi preference: the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is relied upon over Abu Hanifa's stricter stance on stable manure.

Guided Path
1
Identify each impurity scenario
The passage discusses three distinct cases: (1) camel dung (ba'r) in a milking pail, (2) mud from walking, and (3) stable manure (arwath) on the foot. Identify the impurity and the context in which it occurs.
Each has a different ruling based on custom and the nature of the impurity.
2
Determine the legal status of the impurity
For each scenario, state whether the substance is inherently najis (impure) according to the Hanafi school. All three are considered najis, but the ruling on prayer differs.
Camel dung and manure are najis, but mud may contain a mixture of impurities — its ruling is based on the difficulty of avoidance.
3
Identify the dispensation condition
Each case has a condition that must be met for the dispensation to apply: for camel dung, it must be thrown away before disintegrating; for mud, the trace of impurity must not be visible; for manure, the amount must not be excessive (fuhsh).
The term 'fuhsh' is determined by custom (urf), not a fixed measure.
4
State the ruling on prayer validity
If the condition is met, prayer with that impurity is valid (sahih). For camel dung, the milk remains pure. For mud, washing is not obligatory. For manure, prayer is valid as long as the amount is not excessive.
The emphasis on caution in prayer does not override the dispensation.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi preference
In the manure case, the author explicitly states 'وهو ترجيح لقولهما' — this is a preference for the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) over Abu Hanifa, who was stricter. The mufta bihi position is the Sahibayn's view.
This illustrates how later Hanafi authorities (like the author of al-Bazzaziyya) sometimes prefer the Sahibayn over Abu Hanifa based on necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البعر إذا وقع في المحلب عند الحلب فرمي قبل التفتت لا يتنجس. وفي البزازية مشى في الطين أو أصابه لا يجب في الحكم غسله، ولو صلى به جاز ما لم يتبين أثر النجاسة والاحتياط في الصلاة التي هي وجه دينه ومفاتيح رزقه وأول ما يسأل في الموقف وأول منزلة الآخرة لا غاية له، ولهذا قلنا: حمل المصلي أي السجادة أولى من تركه في زماننا دخل مربطاً وأصاب رجله الأرواث، جازت الصلاة معه ما لم يفحش .اهـ. وهو ترجيح لقولهما في الأرواث كما لا يخفى.
Camel dung: if it falls into the milking pail during milking and is thrown away before it disintegrates, it does not become impure. And in al-Bazzaziyya: if one walks in mud or it touches him, it is not obligatory in judgment to wash it, and if he prays with it, it is permissible as long as the trace of impurity is not evident. And caution in prayer, which is the face of his religion, the keys of his sustenance, the first thing asked about in the standing, and the first stage of the afterlife, has no limit. That is why we said: the praying person carrying a prayer mat is better than leaving it. In our time, if he enters a stable and his foot gets manure, his prayer is permissible as long as it is not excessive. That is a preference for the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding dung, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings from the Hanafi school on impurities related to animal droppings and mud. First, the ruling on camel dung (بعر) that falls into a milking pail: if it is thrown away quickly before it dissolves, the milk remains pure (non-impure). This is a specific dispensation because of common occurrence. Second, from al-Bazzaziyya: if one's clothes or body get muddy from walking on a dirty road, it is not legally obligatory to wash it unless the impurity is visible. This does not mean the mud is pure, but the law does not require washing due to custom and difficulty. If one prays with such mud, it is valid as long as there is no visible trace of impurity. The author emphasizes the importance of caution in prayer but recognizes practical difficulty. Third, the ruling on stable manure: if one enters a stable and his foot gets manure on it, his prayer is valid unless the amount is excessive (فحش). This is a preference (ترجيح) for the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) over Abu Hanifa, who may have considered it more strictly. The relied-upon position is that a small amount of manure is excused in prayer.

Key Terms
بعر (baʿr): Camel dung; considered impure but with dispensation when fresh and removed quickly.
أرواث (arwāth): Plural of روث (dung); here refers to stable manure.
فحش (fuḥsh): Excessive amount; threshold determined by customary assessment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if camel dung falls into the milk during milking and is thrown away before it disintegrates, what is the ruling on the milk?
A) It becomes impure and cannot be used.
B) It remains pure and permissible to use.
C) It is makruh to use.
D) It depends on the amount of dung.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants practical dispensations for common impurities like camel dung, mud, and manure, provided they are not excessive or visibly present.
