Specific Rulings on Impurities: Snake Skin, Worms, Donkey, Wind, Vapors, and Sewage
Lists specific rulings on impurities from snake skin, worms, donkey, wind, vapors, and sewage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on each of the listed impurities and identify the key principle (e.g., origin, transmission, certainty) that governs each ruling.

Core Idea
A snake's shed skin is pure, but its skin is not; worms from private parts are impure, but from meat are pure; donkeys make juice impure; wind and vapors do not transfer impurity unless smell is detected; sewage requires washing only if believed impure.

In this lesson, we examine a series of concise rulings from the Hanafi school on various types of impurities and their transmission. These rulings illustrate key principles: the origin of impurity, the effect of tanning, and the role of certainty in determining contamination.

Guided Path
1
Snake Skin and Its Shed Skin
Identify that the actual skin of a snake is impure even if slaughtered, because it cannot be purified by tanning. However, the shed skin (qamis) is pure because it is not part of the living snake's skin.
Tanning does not affect snake skin due to its nature.
2
Worms from Different Origins
A worm that falls from the private parts (urethra or anus) is impure because it originates from an impure source. A worm found in meat is pure because its origin is not impure.
Origin of impurity is key.
3
Donkey Drinking Juice
If a donkey drinks from juice, the juice becomes impure and is not permissible to drink, because the donkey is an impure animal.
Impurity transfers via contact with an impure animal.
4
Wind Passing Over Filth
If wind passes over filth and hits wet clothing, the clothing becomes impure only if the smell of impurity is detected. Without smell, it remains pure.
Impurity transfer requires evidence of contact.
5
Vapors and Sewage Splashes
Vapors rising from impurities do not make clothing impure (the correct view). If liquid from a cesspit splashes onto clothing, washing is recommended but not obligatory unless one's stronger opinion is that it is impure.
Certainty governs obligation to wash.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جلد الحية نجس وإن كانت مذبوحة لأن جلدها لا يحتمل الدباغة بخلاف قميصها فإنه طاهر، والدودة الساقطة من السبيلين نجسة بخلاف الساقطة من اللحم فإنها طاهرة الحمار إذا شرب من العصير لا يجوز شربه، الريح إذا مرت بالعذرات وأصابت الثوب المبلول يتنجس إن وجدت رائحة النجاسة فيه، وما يصيب الثوب من بخارات النجاسات قيل يتنجس الثوب بها وقيل لا يتنجس وهو الصحيح. ولو أصاب الثوب ما سال من الكنيف فالأحب أن يغسله ولا يجب ما لم يكن أكبر رأيه أنه نجس.
The skin of a snake is impure even if it is slaughtered, because its skin cannot be tanned, unlike its outer garment (shed skin) which is pure. The worm that falls from the two passages (penis and anus) is impure, unlike the worm that falls from meat, for it is pure. If a donkey drinks from juice, it is not permissible to drink it. If wind passes over filth and hits wet clothing, it becomes impure if the smell of impurity is found in it. As for what strikes the clothing from the vapors of impurities, it is said the clothing becomes impure, and it is said it does not become impure, and the latter is correct. And if what flows from a cesspit touches clothing, it is preferable to wash it, and it is not obligatory unless his stronger opinion is that it is impure.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage delivers a series of specific rulings on various types of impurities. 1) Snake skin: the actual skin of a snake is impure even if the snake is slaughtered because its skin is not receptive to tanning (unlike other animals whose skin becomes pure after tanning). However, the 'qamis' (the outer shed skin) is pure because it is not part of the living snake's skin. 2) Worms: a worm that exits from the private parts (urinary or anal) is impure because it originates from an impure source. But a worm found inside meat (such as in fruits or animal flesh) is pure, as it is not considered an impurity itself. 3) Donkey drinking juice: if a donkey drinks from fruit juice, the juice becomes impure (as the donkey is an impure animal), so it is forbidden to drink it. 4) Wind over filth: if wind blows over impurities and hits wet clothing, the clothing becomes impure if the smell of impurity is detected on it. 5) Vapors: there is disagreement over vapors rising from impurities: some say they make clothing impure, but the correct view is that they do not, as vapors are not substantial impurities. 6) Sewage flow: if liquid from a toilet or cesspit splashes onto clothing, it is recommended (mustahabb) to wash it, but not obligatory, unless one believes it is likely impure.

Key Terms
قميصها (qamīṣuhā): The snake's shed skin; considered pure unlike the actual skin.
سبيلين (sabīlayn): The two passages: urethra and anus.
كنيف (kanīf): Cesspit or toilet area from which sewage may flow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the shed skin (قميص) of a snake according to the passage?
A) It is impure because the snake is impure.
B) It is pure because it is not part of the living snake.
C) It is pure only if the snake was slaughtered.
D) It is makruh to touch.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, impurity is determined by origin and transmission; certainty is required for obligation of washing.

Rulings on Human Body Parts and Animal Teeth: Skin, Nail, Bone, Ear, Tooth, Dead Body
States rulings on impurity of human body parts and teeth with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on impurity of human skin, bone, ear, tooth, and animal parts, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Human skin and bone are impure; cut ear and tooth are pure for the owner per Abu Yusuf, but impure per Muhammad.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings on the impurity or purity of various human body parts and animal parts when they fall into water or become detached. The rulings are nuanced, with distinctions between moist and dry skin, between the owner and another person, and between different types of teeth. The text also highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding human bone, ear, and tooth. Understanding these details is essential for determining the validity of wudu and prayer when such parts are encountered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and condition
For each part (skin, nail, bone, ear, tooth, nerve), note whether it is moist/dry, large/small, and if detached from the owner or another.
Moisture and size matter for water spoilage.
2
Apply the general rule for little water
Recall that little water is spoiled by a significant impurity (size of a dirham or fingernail). Check if the object meets that threshold.
Little water is water less than two qullahs.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi disagreement
For bone, ear, and tooth, note that Abu Yusuf considers them pure for the owner, while Muhammad considers them impure if larger than a dirham.
Bone: Abu Yusuf says pure; others say impure. Ear/Tooth: Abu Yusuf says pure; Muhammad says impure.
4
Trace the tarjih (preferred view)
The text adopts Muhammad's view for the ear ("به نأخذ"), but later commentary indicates the sound view (zahir al-madhhab) is that teeth are pure.
Tarjih: for ear, Muhammad; for teeth, the sound view favors purity.
5
Apply to special cases
Consider separate rulings: dog and fox teeth are pure; dog skin is impure but hair is pure; mouth water of a dead person is impure, of a sleeping person is pure.
These are from the same passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جلدة آدمي إذا وقعت في الماء القليل تفسده إذا كانت قدر الظفر، والظفر لو وقع بنفسه لا يفسده الكافر الميت نجس قبل الغسل وبعده وكذلك الميت، وعظم الآدمي نجس، وعن أبي يوسف أنه طاهر والأذن المقطوعة والسن المقلوعة طاهرتان في حق صاحبهما وإن كانتا أكثر من قدر الدرهم وهذا قول أبي يوسف وقال محمد في الأسنان الساقطة: إنها نجسة وإن كانت أكثر من قدر الدرهم وفي قياس قوله الأذن نجس وبه نأخذ. وقال محمد في صلاة الأثر : سن وقعت في الماء القليل يفسد وإذا طحنت في الحنطة لا تؤكل . وعن أبي يتغير طعمه يؤكل أيضاً اهـ . اهـ. قوله : (جلدة الآدمي إذا وقعت في الماء القليل الخ) قال ابن أمير حاج : وإن كان دونه لا يفسده صرح به غير واحد من أعيان المشايخ ومنهم من عبر بأنه إن كان كثيراً أفسده وإن كان قليلاً يفسده، وأفاد أن الكثير ما كان مقدار الظفر، وأن القليل ما دونه . ثم في محيط الشيخ رضى الدين تعليلاً لفساد الماء بالكثير لأن هذا من حملة لحم الآدمي وقد بان من الحي فيكون نجساً إلا أن في القليل تعذر الاحتراز عنه فلم يفسد الماء لأجل الضرورة. وفيه قبل هذا قال محمد: عصب الميتة وجلدها إذا يبس فوقع في الماء لا يفسده لأن باليبس زالت عنه الرطوبة النجسة اهـ. ومشى عليه في الملتقط من غير عز وإلى أحد، فعلى هذا ينبغي تقييد جلد الآدمي الكثير في هذه المسألة بكونه رطباً . ثم لا يخفى أن فساد الماء به بعد ذلك مقيد بكونه قليلاً. اهـ من كلام ابن أمير البحر الرائق يوسف أن سنه طاهر في حقه حتى إذا أثبتها جازت الصلاة وإن أثبت سن غيره لا يجوز وقال : بينهما فرق وإن لم يحضرني. وسن الكلب والثعلب طاهرة وجلد الكلب نجس وشعره طاهر وهو المختار وماء فم الميت نجس بخلاف ماء فم النائم فإنه طاهر ا هـ .
The skin of a human: if it falls into little water, it spoils it if it is the size of a fingernail. As for a nail falling by itself, it does not spoil it. The dead disbeliever is impure before washing and after, and similarly the dead (Muslim). The bone of a human is impure, and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that it is pure. And the cut ear and the extracted tooth are pure in relation to their owner, even if they are more than a dirham in size – this is the statement of Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said regarding fallen teeth: they are impure, even if they are more than a dirham, and by analogy his statement implies the ear is impure, and we adopt this. And Muhammad said in the 'Prayer of Relics': a tooth that falls into little water spoils it, and if it is ground into wheat, it is not eaten. And from Abu (Yusuf): if its taste changes, it is also eaten. His statement: (the skin of a human if it falls into little water) – Ibn Amir Hajj said: and if it is less than that, it does not spoil it, as explicitly stated by many leading scholars, and some of them expressed that if it is much it spoils it, and if it is little it spoils it? (ambiguous) and it indicated that 'much' is the size of a fingernail, and 'little' is less. Then in the Muhit of Shaykh Rad al-Din, an explanation for the spoilage of water by a large amount because this is from flesh of a human that has separated from the living, so it is impure, except that in a small amount, avoidance is difficult, so it does not spoil the water due to necessity. And in it before this, Muhammad said: the nerve (عصب) of a dead animal and its skin, if they dry and fall into water, do not spoil it, because with drying the impure moisture is removed. And it is followed in al-Multaqat without attribution, and according to this, the large human skin in this issue should be restricted to being moist. Then it is not hidden that the spoilage of water by it is then restricted to it being little water. End of statement of Ibn Amir from al-Bahr al-Raiq. Yusuf: his tooth is pure in his right, such that if he fixes it, prayer is permissible, but if he fixes the tooth of another, it is not permissible, and he said: there is a difference between them, though I do not have it present. And the tooth of a dog and fox is pure, and the skin of a dog is impure, and its hair is pure – this is the chosen view. And the water of a dead person's mouth is impure, unlike the water of a sleeping person's mouth, for it is pure.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage deals with the impurity or purity of various human body parts and animal teeth, with notable differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The rulings can be summarized as follows: (1) Human skin (جلدة) that falls into a small amount of water spoils the water if the skin is the size of a fingernail; if it falls by itself (e.g., a nail), it does not spoil. (2) A dead human (Muslim or disbeliever) is impure before and after washing. (3) Human bone: according to the dominant view, it is impure, but Abu Yusuf considers it pure. (4) Cut ear and extracted tooth: pure for the owner according to Abu Yusuf, even if large; Muhammad considers them impure if beyond a dirham in size. The text says we adopt Muhammad's view for the ear (and by analogy tooth). (5) Human tooth fallen into water: if it spoils the water, and if ground into wheat, the wheat becomes impermissible to eat. (6) The commentary by Ibn Amir Hajj explains that the human skin ruling is qualified: only if the skin is moist and in little water does it spoil; if dry, it does not. (7) Dog and fox teeth are pure; dog skin is impure but its hair is pure. (8) Mouth water of a dead person is impure; that of a sleeping person is pure. The intra-Hanafi dispute is evident on bone, ear, and tooth.

Key Terms
جلدة آدمي (jildat ādamī): Human skin; if moist and size of fingernail, spoils little water.
درهم (dirham): A unit of area, roughly the size of a silver dirham coin (approx. 2.5 cm diameter); used as threshold for impurity.
عصب (ʿaṣab): Nerve or sinew; its impurity is disputed (two narrations).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on the tooth of a dog?
A) It is impure like the skin of a dog.
B) It is pure.
C) It is pure only if less than a dirham.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
Human skin, bone, and ear (detached) are impure, but the sound Hanafi view considers a person's own tooth pure, regardless of size.

Ruling on Moisture After Istinja and Passing Wind
Later fatwa ruling on moisture impurity and prayer with animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the chosen view (mukhtar) regarding the impurity of moisture after istinja and wind, and explain why the majority disagreed.

Core Idea
The chosen view: moisture after istinja becomes impure if one passes wind without drying.

This passage from al-Khulasa presents a dispute among later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) concerning a hypothetical: a person performs istinja with water, does not wipe dry, then passes wind. Does the remaining moisture become impure? The majority said no; the chosen (mukhtar) view says yes. The same applies to sweat on trousers. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between pure moisture and moisture that has been in contact with an impure source. The lesson will also touch on the permissibility of praying while carrying mice, cats, or snakes, and their leftover water, as mentioned in the same passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is istinja with water, followed by not drying, then passing wind. The dispute: does the moisture become impure? Majority say no, chosen view (mukhtar) says yes. Also the similar case of sweat on trousers without istinja.
The mukhtar is the view of the later jurists, not necessarily the founding imams.
2
Understand the ruling on praying with animals
The passage says prayer is valid but makruh when carrying a mouse, cat, or snake. Also their leftover water (sūr) is permissible for wudu, though there is disagreement. This is a separate ruling but included here.
This shows that minor impurities or carries do not invalidate prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

موافق للمنقول عن ظاهر الرواية والتفرقة بينهما على غير ظاهر الرواية . قال العلامة الحلبي في شرحه الكبير : وأما الآدمي فإن كان سن نفسه تجوز الصلاة معه وإن زاد على قدر الدرهم عند أبي يوسف. وقال محمد لا تجوز إذا زاد على قدر الدرهم وإن كان سن غيره وزاد على قدر الدرهم لا تجوز بالاتفاق لكن هذا كله على القول بنجاسة السن على تقدير أنه طرف عصب، وفي نجاسة العصب روايتان قاله في الكفاية. قال فيها وعلى ظاهر المسمى بالعرقي في ولاية الروم نجس في
And in al-Khulasa: If one performs istinja with water and does not wipe it with a towel until he passes wind, the scholars differed: most scholars say it does not become impure, but the chosen view is that it does become impure. Likewise, if he did not perform istinja but his trousers became wet with sweat or water and then he passed wind. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: The water of a roof (ماء المطابق) is impure by analogy but is not impure by istihsan as long as no trace of impurity appears in it. Similarly, a stable if it is hot and there is a roof above its dome, or a sewer house with a roof from which it drips. Similarly, a bathhouse if impurities are poured in it and the walls and dome sweat and drip. Similarly, if a hanging pitcher in a stable contains water and moisture seeps at the bottom of the pitcher: by analogy it becomes impure because the moisture at the bottom became impure by the vapor of the stable, but by istihsan it does not become impure because the pitcher is pure and the water in it is pure, so what seeps from it is pure. If one prays while carrying a mouse, cat, or snake, his prayer is valid but he has done wrong, and similarly it is permissible to perform wudu with its leftover water (سؤره), though there is difference. The statement: (the tooth of a dog and fox is pure) – al-Khayr al-Ramli said: ponder it with their statement 'whatever is separated from a living animal, even a tooth', for its implication is the impurity of the tooth of a dog and fox, and also the statement of its purity and the impurity of a human tooth later. And I say: regarding the impurity of a tooth, there is a problem: it cannot be but that it is either bone or nerve, and both are pure. As for bone, there is no disagreement among us; as for nerve, it is according to the well-known (mashhur) position of the school. In Fath al-Qadir, there is a report of no impurity in it, although the author of al-Bahr looked into it, and the correct view is that they (bone and nerve) should be treated the same. Contemplate this. End. I say: his problem is not valid, and what he researched with his saying 'and what' is in agreement with that transmitted from the apparent (zahir) narration, and the distinction between them is not based on the apparent narration. Al-Allama al-Halabi said in his large commentary: As for a human, if it is his own tooth, prayer is permissible with it even if it exceeds a dirham, according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said it is not permissible if it exceeds a dirham. And if it is the tooth of another and exceeds a dirham, it is not permissible by agreement. But all this is based on the saying that the tooth is impure on the assumption that it is a nerve tip. Regarding the impurity of nerve, there are two narrations, as stated in al-Kifaya. He said: according to the apparent (zahir) of the school, which is correct, there is no disagreement among our scholars that a tooth is pure, and the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is based on the narration that the bone of teeth is impure. End. Similarly in al-Kafi. End. So the problem is resolved: it is based on one of two narrations regarding nerve. His statement: (and the chosen view is that it becomes impure) will come later that the fatwa is contrary to it. His statement: (and it is not impure by istihsan) – al-Allama al-Halabi said: the apparent basis of istihsan here is necessity due to the impossibility of avoidance or its difficulty, since there is no text or consensus on the matter, and the grounds of istihsan are limited to these three. Based on this, if an impurity is distilled, its liquid is impure, unlike its other parts because necessity is absent, so analogy remains without objection. And from this it is known that what is distilled from wine dregs called 'araq in the land of Rum (Anatolia) is impure.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage combines late fatwa rulings and a super-commentary that resolves earlier disputes. 1) Istinja and passing wind: The chosen view (mukhtar) is that if one does not dry after istinja and then passes wind, the moisture becomes impure, but the majority said it does not. Similarly for sweat. 2) Roof water (ماء المطابق): By strict analogy, water that condenses from vapors of impurities on a roof should be impure, but by istihsan (juristic preference) it is considered pure unless a trace of impurity appears. This applies to stables, bathhouses, and sewer houses. 3) Praying with a mouse, cat, or snake in one's pocket or sleeve: the prayer is valid but disliked (اساءة). The leftover water (سؤر) of these animals is permissible for wudu according to one view. 4) Commentary on dog tooth: Al-Khayr al-Ramli notes a contradiction: the rule 'whatever is separated from a living animal is impure' would make dog teeth impure, yet the text says they are pure. The author of al-Bahr resolves this by noting that teeth are either bone or nerve, both of which are pure in the Hanafi school. The apparent narration (ظاهر الرواية) at the heart of the school (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that there is no impurity in teeth, and the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is based on a weak narration. Therefore, the correct view is that human teeth are pure, and the prayer is valid even with a tooth larger than a dirham (per Abu Yusuf). 5) Istihsan basis: The reason for the istihsan in the roof water case is necessity (ضرورة), as it is difficult to avoid. This principle is then applied to the distillation of alcohol: the distilled liquid (e.g., 'araq) remains impure because there is no necessity to consume it.

Key Terms
استنجى (istinjā): Cleansing the private parts with water after using the toilet.
فسا (fasā): Passing wind (flatulence) from the rear.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The chosen view among later scholars, often after weighing evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the chosen view (mukhtar) regarding moisture after istinja and passing wind?
A) It remains pure.
B) It becomes impure.
C) It is doubtful.
D) It depends on the amount of water.

Key Takeaway
Later Hanafi scholars differed on whether moisture after istinja becomes impure upon passing wind; the mukhtar says yes, but the operative fatwa may follow the majority.

Roof Condensation Water and Istihsān
Fatwa on roof water and istihsān examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why roof condensation water is ruled pure by istihsān despite being impure by analogy, and apply the same rationale to similar cases like stable vapors and bathhouse sweat.

Core Idea
Water condensing from impure vapors is pure by istihsān unless impurity trace is visible.

This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan presents a classic Hanafi application of istihsān: by strict analogy (qiyas), water that condenses on a roof from vapors rising from impurities (like a sewer) should be impure because it originates from an impure source. However, by istihsān (juristic preference), it is ruled pure unless a trace of impurity actually appears in the water. The reason is necessity (ḍarūrah): it is nearly impossible to avoid such condensation in daily life. The same ruling applies to water dripping from stable domes, bathhouse walls, and pitcher seepage. The lesson also extends to the distillation of impure substances: the liquid distillate remains impure if there is no necessity.

Guided Path
1
State the initial analogy (qiyas)
Understand the qiyas: the vapor rises from impurity, so the condensed water is deemed impure by analogy with transferring impurity via moisture. This would make all such water impure.
2
Identify the istihsān exception
The Hanafi school applies istihsān: the water is pure as long as no trace of impurity is visible. The basis is necessity (ḍarūrah) because it is difficult to avoid.
Istihsān here overrides qiyas because qiyas leads to hardship.
3
List the parallel cases
The passage gives examples: a hot stable with a roof, a sewer house with a roof, a bathhouse where impure water is poured and walls sweat, and a hanging pitcher in a stable. In all, the ruling by istihsān is purity.
The common factor: vapors from impurities condense or seep but no direct contact.
4
Apply the principle to distillation
The passage then applies the rule: if an impurity is distilled, the liquid (spirit) is impure because there is no necessity; people can avoid it. The solid dregs remain impure as well? Actually the text says the liquid is impure, unlike other parts because necessity is absent.
This distinguishes between water condensation (necessary) and alcohol distillation (not necessary).

Human Tooth Purity: Resolving the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Super-commentary resolving tooth purity dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling on the purity of a human tooth and explain how the commentator resolved the apparent contradiction between earlier texts.

Core Idea
The correct Hanafi position: a human tooth is pure, resolving the Abu Yusuf-Muhammad dispute as based on a weak narration.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a super-commentary that resolves a long-standing intra-Hanafi disagreement about the purity of a human tooth. Earlier, the matn stated that the tooth of a dog and fox is pure, which seemed to contradict the general principle that anything severed from a living animal is impure (including teeth). Al-Khayr al-Ramli noted this difficulty. The commentator (the author of al-Bahr) explains that teeth are either bone or nerve, both ruled pure in the school. The dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the impurity of human teeth is based on a weak narration regarding the nerve. Therefore, the sound and apparent position (zahir al-madhhab) is that the human tooth is pure, and the prayer is valid even with a tooth larger than a dirham (according to Abu Yusuf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The matn states dog and fox teeth are pure. But the general rule says everything severed from a living animal is impure. So there is an apparent contradiction. Also human teeth are mentioned later with disagreement.
The commentator first flags the inconsistency.
2
Recognize the commentator's resolution
The commentator argues that teeth are either bone or nerve. Both are pure in the Hanafi school (bone is pure without dispute; nerve is pure on the well-known view). Therefore, the teeth are pure. The disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is based on a weak narration that treats nerve as impure.
So the contradiction is resolved by noting that teeth are not like other body parts; they are akin to bone.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator cites al-Kifaya and al-Kafi: the correct view is that there is no disagreement among Hanafi scholars that the tooth is pure; the reported dispute is based on a weak narration. Therefore, a human tooth is pure regardless of size, and prayer with it is valid even if it exceeds a dirham (Abu Yusuf's view).
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

And I say: regarding the impurity of a tooth, there is a problem: it cannot be but that it is either bone or nerve, and both are pure. As for bone, there is no disagreement among us; as for nerve, it is according to the well-known (mashhur) position of the school. In Fath al-Qadir, there is a report of no impurity in it, although the author of al-Bahr looked into it, and the correct view is that they (bone and nerve) should be treated the same. Contemplate this. End. I say: his problem is not valid, and what he researched with his saying 'and what' is in agreement with that transmitted from the apparent (zahir) narration, and the distinction between them is not based on the apparent narration. Al-Allama al-Halabi said in his large commentary: As for a human, if it is his own tooth, prayer is permissible with it even if it exceeds a dirham, according to Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said it is not permissible if it exceeds a dirham. And if it is the tooth of another and exceeds a dirham, it is not permissible by agreement. But all this is based on the saying that the tooth is impure on the assumption that it is a nerve tip. Regarding the impurity of nerve, there are two narrations, as stated in al-Kifaya. He said: according to the apparent (zahir) of the school, which is correct, there is no disagreement among our scholars that a tooth is pure, and the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is based on the narration that the bone of teeth is impure. End. Similarly in al-Kafi. End. So the problem is resolved: it is based on one of two narrations regarding nerve. His statement: (and the chosen view is that it becomes impure) will come later that the fatwa is contrary to it. His statement: (and it is not impure by istihsan) – al-Allama al-Halabi said: the apparent basis of istihsan here is necessity due to the impossibility of avoidance or its difficulty, since there is no text or consensus on the matter, and the grounds of istihsan are limited to these three. Based on this, if an impurity is distilled, its liquid is impure, unlike its other parts because necessity is absent, so analogy remains without objection. And from this it is known that what is distilled from wine dregs called 'araq in the land of Rum (Anatolia) is impure.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage combines late fatwa rulings and a super-commentary that resolves earlier disputes. 1) Istinja and passing wind: The chosen view (mukhtar) is that if one does not dry after istinja and then passes wind, the moisture becomes impure, but the majority said it does not. Similarly for sweat. 2) Roof water (ماء المطابق): By strict analogy, water that condenses from vapors of impurities on a roof should be impure, but by istihsan (juristic preference) it is considered pure unless a trace of impurity appears. This applies to stables, bathhouses, and sewer houses. 3) Praying with a mouse, cat, or snake in one's pocket or sleeve: the prayer is valid but disliked (اساءة). The leftover water (سؤر) of these animals is permissible for wudu according to one view. 4) Commentary on dog tooth: Al-Khayr al-Ramli notes a contradiction: the rule 'whatever is separated from a living animal is impure' would make dog teeth impure, yet the text says they are pure. The author of al-Bahr resolves this by noting that teeth are either bone or nerve, both of which are pure in the Hanafi school. The apparent narration (ظاهر الرواية) at the heart of the school (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that there is no impurity in teeth, and the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is based on a weak narration. Therefore, the correct view is that human teeth are pure, and the prayer is valid even with a tooth larger than a dirham (per Abu Yusuf). 5) Istihsan basis: The reason for the istihsan in the roof water case is necessity (ضرورة), as it is difficult to avoid. This principle is then applied to the distillation of alcohol: the distilled liquid (e.g., 'araq) remains impure because there is no necessity to consume it.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The apparent/well-known narration from the three founding imams.
عصب (ʿaṣab): Nerve; some narrations considered this impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the basis for the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on human tooth impurity?
A) Whether the tooth is owned or not.
B) Whether the tooth is from nerve or bone.
C) The size of the tooth.
D) Whether the tooth is from a believer or not.

Key Takeaway
The correct Hanafi view: human teeth are pure, and prayer with them is valid; the Abu Yusuf-Muhammad disagreement is based on a weak narration.

Ruling on Fox or Puppy in the Sleeve
Ruling statement with cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding a fox or puppy in one's sleeve during prayer and identify the reason for invalidity.

Core Idea
The leftover water of a predatory animal is impure, invalidating prayer.

The Hanafi school holds that the leftover water (saur) of predatory animals is impure (najis). If a fox or puppy is in one's sleeve, its saur contaminates the garment, rendering the prayer invalid. This lesson examines that ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act under consideration is prayer (salat).
2
Identify the condition
A fox or puppy is present in the sleeve of the worshipper.
The animal's presence is not the issue; its leftover water is.
3
Determine the ruling
The prayer is not permissible (la tujuz).
Invalidity means the prayer is not valid.
4
Identify the cause
The cause is the animal's leftover water (saur) being impure (najis).
Saur refers to water or drink leftover from the animal's mouth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان في كمه ثعلب أو جرو كلب لا تجوز صلاته لأن سؤره نجس. ثوب أصابه عصير ومضى على ذلك أيام جازت الصلاة فيه عند علمائنا لأنه لا يصير خمراً في الثوب، والمسك حلال على كل حال يؤكل في الطعام ويجعل الأدوية ولا يقال إن المسك دم لأنها وإن كانت دماً فقد تغيرت فيصير طاهراً كرماد العذرة.
If there is a fox or a puppy in his sleeve, his prayer is not permissible because its leftover water (saur) is impure. A garment that grape juice fell on and days passed, prayer is permissible in it according to our scholars because it does not become khamr in the garment. Musk is permissible in all cases, it is eaten in food and used in medicines, and it is not said that musk is blood because even if it was blood, it has changed and thus becomes pure like the ash of dung.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage contains three distinct rulings on impurities related to garments. First, if a person has a fox or puppy in his sleeve, his prayer is invalid because the animal's saliva (saur) is considered najis (impure) according to the Hanafi school. This is based on the general principle that the leftover water of predatory animals is impure. Second, if grape juice falls on a garment and days pass without it turning into wine, the garment remains pure and prayer is valid in it. The reason is that the juice does not become khamr (wine) while on the garment; wine is only considered such when it becomes intoxicating. Third, musk is completely halal and pure, even though it originates from blood, because the blood has transformed (istihala) into a different substance, similar to how the ash of burned impure matter becomes pure.

Key Terms
سؤره (saur): Leftover water or drink from an animal's mouth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a fox in the sleeve invalidate prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the fox is najis al-ayn
B) Because its saur is impure
C) Because it distracts the worshipper
D) Because touching a fox breaks wudu

Key Takeaway
The saur of predatory animals like foxes and dogs is impure, so prayer with such animals in or on one's garment is invalid.

Ruling on Grape Juice on a Garment
Ruling statement with cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why grape juice on a garment does not invalidate prayer as long as it does not become wine.

Core Idea
Grape juice stays pure on a garment unless it ferments into wine.

Grape juice itself is pure, but if it becomes wine (khamr), it becomes impure. The Hanafi school rules that juice on a garment does not automatically become wine; therefore, prayer remains valid. This lesson explains the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Grape juice falls on a garment, and days pass without the garment being washed.
The passage says 'days passed' indicating a significant time.
2
State the ruling
Prayer in that garment is permissible (jaza'at).
This is the Hanafi position.
3
Understand the reasoning
The juice does not become khamr (wine) while on the garment.
Khamr is a specific intoxicant; grape juice alone is not najis.
4
Implication
Since the impurity of wine never formed, the garment remains pure.
If it had turned into wine, the garment would become impure and require washing.

Source Text

كان في كمه ثعلب أو جرو كلب لا تجوز صلاته لأن سؤره نجس. ثوب أصابه عصير ومضى على ذلك أيام جازت الصلاة فيه عند علمائنا لأنه لا يصير خمراً في الثوب، والمسك حلال على كل حال يؤكل في الطعام ويجعل الأدوية ولا يقال إن المسك دم لأنها وإن كانت دماً فقد تغيرت فيصير طاهراً كرماد العذرة.
If there is a fox or a puppy in his sleeve, his prayer is not permissible because its leftover water (saur) is impure. Musk is permissible in all cases, it is eaten in food and used in medicines, and it is not said that musk is blood because even if it was blood, it has changed and thus becomes pure like the ash of dung.

Commentary
This passage contains three distinct rulings on impurities related to garments. First, if a person has a fox or puppy in his sleeve, his prayer is invalid because the animal's saliva (saur) is considered najis (impure) according to the Hanafi school. Second, if grape juice falls on a garment and days pass without it turning into wine, the garment remains pure and prayer is valid in it. The reason is that the juice does not become khamr (wine) while on the garment; wine is only considered such when it becomes intoxicating. Third, musk is completely halal and pure, even though it originates from blood, because the blood has transformed (istihala) into a different substance, similar to how the ash of burned impure matter becomes pure.

Key Terms
العصير (al-'aseer): Grape juice or any fruit juice, which is pure as long as it does not become intoxicating.
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine, an intoxicating beverage made from grapes, which is impure (najis) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Musk: Purity Through Transformation
Ruling statement with analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the principle of istihala (transformation) and apply it to conclude that musk is pure (tahir) and permissible.

Core Idea
Musk is pure because its original blood has transformed into a different substance through istihala.

Musk is a substance from the musk deer's gland that originally contains blood, but through a transformation process (istihala) it becomes a different, pure substance. The Hanafi school thus considers musk completely halal and pure, usable in food and medicine. This lesson explains the reasoning and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substance
Musk (al-misk), a fragrant substance from the musk deer.
It is often used in perfumes.
2
Recognize the potential impurity
Musk originates from a type of blood, which is normally impure.
Blood is najis in the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the principle of istihala
The blood has changed into a different substance with different properties.
Istihala means complete transformation into a different thing.
4
Give the analogy
It is like the ash of burned dung: the original impurity is transformed into pure ash.
The text explicitly compares musk to ramad al-'adhara (ash of dung).
5
State the ruling
Musk is pure (tahir) and permissible (halal) in all uses.
It can be eaten, used in medicines, etc.

Source Text

If there is a fox or a puppy in his sleeve, his prayer is not permissible because its leftover water (saur) is impure. A garment that grape juice fell on and days passed, prayer is permissible in it according to our scholars because it does not become khamr in the garment. Musk is permissible in all cases, it is eaten in food and used in medicines, and it is not said that musk is blood because even if it was blood, it has changed and thus becomes pure like the ash of dung.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage contains three distinct rulings on impurities related to garments. First, if a person has a fox or puppy in his sleeve, his prayer is invalid because the animal's saliva (saur) is considered najis (impure) according to the Hanafi school. This is based on the general principle that the leftover water of predatory animals is impure. Second, if grape juice falls on a garment and days pass without it turning into wine, the garment remains pure and prayer is valid in it. The reason is that the juice does not become khamr (wine) while on the garment; wine is only considered such when it becomes intoxicating. Third, musk is completely halal and pure, even though it originates from blood, because the blood has transformed (istihala) into a different substance, similar to how the ash of burned impure matter becomes pure.

Key Terms
المسك (al-misk): Musk, a fragrant substance from the musk deer, considered pure due to transformation.
الاستحالة (al-istihala): Transformation of a substance into another, whereby the ruling of the original is lifted.
رماد العذرة (ramad al-'adhara): Ash of burnt dung, used as an analogy for istihala: impure dung becomes pure ash.

The Ruling on Mud Made from Pure Soil and Impure Water
States the correct ruling that mud becomes impure when soil mixes with impure water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi ruling on the impurity of mud formed by mixing pure soil with impure water, and identify the conflicting fatwa that considers the pure component.

Core Idea
Any mud formed by mixing pure soil with impure water becomes impure — the pure element does not purify the mixture.

When pure soil is mixed with impure water to form mud, a natural question arises: does the soil's purity purify the water, or does the water's impurity contaminate the soil? In this lesson, we examine the correct Hanafi ruling on this matter and a conflicting fatwa that gives weight to the pure component. Understanding this ruling is essential for maintaining ritual purity in everyday situations involving mud and clay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Determine which of the two components (soil or water) is pure and which is impure. The ruling applies when at least one is impure.
If both are pure, the mud is pure.
2
Apply the correct opinion
The correct opinion in the Hanafi school is that the resulting mud is impure, regardless of which component was originally impure. The impurity of one component contaminates the entire mixture.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
3
Note the conflicting fatwa
In al-Bazzaziyya, a different fatwa states that consideration is given to the pure component; if the soil is pure, the mud remains pure despite impure water. This is not the operative view.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Understand the practical effect
According to the correct opinion, impure mud cannot be used for purification (e.g., istinja) or for making objects that require ritual purity.
The conflicting fatwa would permit such use if the pure component dominates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التراب الطاهر إذا جعل طيناً بالماء النجس أو على العكس الصحيح أن الطين نجس أيهما ما كان نجساً. وفي البزازية : الفتوى على أن العبرة للطاهر أيهما كان في مسألة التراب الطاهر إذا جعل طيناً بالماء النجس الثالثة حرام كسائر أصناف الخمر .اهـ.
Pure soil, if it is made into mud with impure water, or vice versa, the correct (opinion) is that the mud is impure, whichever of the two was impure. And in al-Bazzaziyya: The fatwa is that consideration is given to the pure element, whichever it may be, in the issue of pure soil when it is made into mud with impure water. The third (type) is forbidden like all types of khamr.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This lesson presents the ruling on mixing pure soil with impure water. According to the correct opinion in the Hanafi school, the resulting mud is impure regardless of whether the soil or the water is the impure one. However, there is a conflicting fatwa recorded in al-Bazzaziyya that considers the pure element, meaning if the soil is pure, the mud remains pure despite the impure water. The phrase 'the third type is forbidden' likely refers to a classification of impurities, but in context it seems to be a continuation from the Bazzaziyya quote, indicating that the third type of impurity (perhaps wine) is absolutely haram. The correct view remains that the mud is impure.

Key Terms
الطين (al-teen): Mud resulting from mixing soil and water

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Pure because soil is pure
B) Impure
C) Makruh
D) Dependent on the ratio

Key Takeaway
The correct Hanafi ruling: any mud formed by mixing pure soil with impure water is impure, regardless of which component was originally pure.

Transfer of Impurity through Non-Dripping Moisture (Nadwah)
Ruling and detailed analysis of when wetness from an impure surface makes a dry garment impure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the transfer of impurity through non-dripping moisture (nadwah), identify the qiyas used to justify it, and explain the conditions and exceptions.

Core Idea
Mere moisture from an impure surface does not transfer impurity unless it drips or leaves a trace.

Have you ever placed a prayer mat on a damp floor and wondered if the moisture makes it impure? The Hanafi school provides a detailed ruling based on a subtle qiyas (analogy) to the purification process of an impure garment. In this lesson, we examine the conditions under which moisture transfer does or does not transmit impurity, including the key distinction between mere moisture (nadwah) and actual flow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
Read the base ruling: when a dry pure garment is placed on wet impure ground, or a wet impure garment is wrapped in a dry pure one, the pure garment remains pure if the moisture does not drip upon wringing and no trace of color or odor appears. Also note the exception for the wetting agent itself (e.g., urine).
The ruling is conditional on three main points.
2
Understand the qiyas (analogy) used
The text compares this case to the moisture remaining on an impure garment after the third wringing in the purification process. That moisture is excused because it is the endpoint of removal. Here, the beginning moisture on the pure garment is likened to that endpoint.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy).
3
Examine the objection and response
Some objected that analogy to the first wringing is more appropriate since impurity is still present. The response explains that in the established impurity case, impurity is gradually removed; the last trace is excused. In the transfer case, the onset of impurity is compared to that final excused state.
Key: beginnning is treated like the end when both share non-dripping.
4
Note the qualifications and exceptions
The ruling only applies if the wetting agent is water, not the impurity itself (like urine). Also, if color or odor appears, the garment becomes impure regardless of dripping. These are essential conditions.
Do not confuse with cases where the impurity is directly transferred.
5
Consider Ibn al-Humam's critique
Ibn al-Humam argues that if tiny droplets appear upon wringing (even if they don't drip), the garment should be impure due to actual mixing. He suggests conditioning non-impurity on nothing emerging at all.
This represents a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا بسط الثوب الطاهر اليابس على أرض نجسة مبتلة فظهرت البلة في الثوب لكن لم يصر رطاً ولا بحال لو عصر يسيل منه شيء متقاطر لكن موضع الندوة يعرف من سائر المواضع الصحيح أنه لا يصير نجساً، وكذا الولف الثوب النجس في ثوب طاهر والنجس رطب مبتل وظهرت ندوته في الثوب الطاهر لكن لم يصر بحال لو عصر يسيل منه شيء متقاطر لا يصير نجساً ا هـ. قال في المنية : الأصح أنه لا يصير نجساً. قال في شرحها كذا في الخلاصة وكثير ذكره من غير إشارة إلى خلاف وكأن وجهه القياس على ما يبقى من الرطوبة بعد العصر في المرة الثالثة بحيث لا يتقاطر بعد لو عصر لكن يرد أن قياسها على النداوة الباقية بعد العصر في المرة الأولى أولى لوجود النجاسة بكمالها في الثوب الذي سرت منه الرطوبة كما في الذي عصر أول مرة. ويجاب بأن النجاسة إذا كانت ثابتة فزالت بالغسل والعصر شيئاً فشيئاً إلى حد النهاية فهي الرطوبة الباقية بعد عصر يعفى عنها حينئذ، وإذا لم تكن ثابتة فابتدأت بالثوب كما في مسألتنا فما دامت البداية مثل تلك النهاية في عدم التقاطر بالعصر يعفى عنها كما عفي هناك بخلاف ما بعد عصر الأولى والثانية فإنه ليس بنهاية، فالحاصل قياس ابتداء النجاسة فيما هو طاهر على انتهائها فيما كان نجساً فليتأمل. وإذا فهم هذا يجب أن يعلم أن وضع المسألة إنما هو في الثوب المبلول بالماء بخلاف المبلول بعين النجاسة كالبول ونحوه لأن النداوة وحينئذ عين النجاسة وإن لم يقطر بالعصر كما لو عصر الثوب المبلول بالبول ونحوه حتى انقطع التقاطر منه فإنه لا يطهر، وكما بعد العصر في المرة الأولى والثانية، وكذا ينبغي أن تقيد المسألة أيضاً بما إذا لم يظهر في الثوب الطاهر أثر النجاسة من لون أو ريح حتى لو كان المبلول متلوناً بلون أو متكيفاً : بريح فظهر ذلك في الطاهر يجب أن يكون نجساً كما لو غسل ذلك النجس ولم يزل أثره ولم يبلغ حد المشقة حيث لا يحكم بطهارته، فكذا هذا إلحاقاً للبداية بالنهاية هذا. وقال الشيخ كمال الدين بن الهمام لا يخفى أنه قد يحصل ببل الثوب وعصره نبع رؤوس صغار ليس لها قوة السيلان ليصل بعضها ببعض فتقطر بل تقر في مواضع نبعها ثم ترجع إذا حل الثوب، ويبعد في مثله الحكم بطهارة الثوب مع وجود حقيقة المخالط، فالأولى إناطة عدم النجاسة بعدم نبع شيء عند العصر ليكون مجرد ندوة لا بعدم التقاطر .اهـ. وقد نقل هذا الفرع المصنف في مسائل شتى آخر الكتاب وفي الوقاية والنقاية والدرر ومتن الملتقى ومتن التنوير على ما مر
And if a dry pure garment is spread on a wet impure ground, and wetness appears in the garment but it does not become wet such that if wrung something would drip from it, yet the spot of moisture is known from the rest of the garment, the correct (opinion) is that it does not become impure. Likewise, if an impure garment is wrapped in a pure garment, and the impure is wet and its moisture appears in the pure garment but it does not become such that if wrung something would drip, it does not become impure. End. Al-Muniyya said: The correct is that it does not become impure. Its commentator said: Thus in al-Khulasa and many mentioned it without indicating any disagreement. Its apparent basis is analogy to the moisture remaining after the third wringing such that after wringing nothing drips. But it is objected that analogy to the moisture remaining after the first wringing is more appropriate because the impurity is complete in the garment from which the wetness came, like the one wrung the first time. The answer is that when impurity is established, it is removed by washing and wringing gradually to the limit, so the remaining moisture after wringing is excused. And when it is not established but begins in the garment as in our case, as long as the beginning is like that limit in not dripping upon wringing, it is excused as there, unlike after the first and second wringings which are not the limit. The conclusion is analogy of the beginning of impurity in what is pure to its end in what was impure, so reflect. When this is understood, one must know that the case is specifically about a garment moistened with water, not with the very impurity like urine, because the moisture then is the very impurity, even if it does not drip upon wringing, just as when one wrings a garment moistened with urine until dripping stops, it does not become pure. And similarly after the first and second wringing. Likewise, the case should be restricted to when no trace of impurity (color or odor) appears in the pure garment. If the wet garment is colored or has an odor and that appears in the pure garment, it must be impure, just as if that impure was washed and its trace did not go away and did not reach the hardship level, where it is not judged pure. So this is attaching the beginning to the end. Sheikh Kamal al-Din ibn al-Humam said: It is not hidden that when a garment is wetted and wrung, tiny points may appear that lack the force to flow and connect to each other to drip, but they stay at their points and return when the garment is released. It is far-fetched in such a case to judge the garment pure with the existence of actual mixing. So the better view is to condition non-impurity on nothing emerging when wrung, so it is mere moisture, not on non-dripping. This subsidiary issue has been transmitted by the author in various places at the end of the book, and in al-Wiqaya, al-Niqaya, al-Durar, the text of al-Multaqa, and the text of al-Tanwir, as mentioned.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This lengthy passage examines a critical issue in Hanafi fiqh: when does moisture from an impure source render a pure garment impure? The base ruling states that if a dry pure garment is placed on a wet impure surface, and wetness appears but does not drip upon wringing, the garment remains pure. The same applies if a wet impure garment is wrapped in a dry pure one. The commentary explores the reasoning: it draws an analogy to the moisture remaining after the third wash of an impure garment—that moisture is excused because after the final wringing, no impurity remains. Some objected that analogy to the first wash is stronger, but the response clarifies that in the established impurity case, the impurity is gradually removed, so the final moisture is excused. In the initial transfer case, the impurity begins here, so as long as the beginning matches the end in non-dripping, it is excused. Important qualifications: this only applies if the wetting agent is water, not the very impurity (like urine), because then any transfer is impure even if it doesn't drip. Also, if color or odor appears, the garment becomes impure. Ibn al-Humam critiques the ruling: he argues that even without dripping, if small droplets emerge upon wringing, the garment should be impure due to actual mixing. He suggests that the condition should be that nothing emerges at all. The author notes that this issue is cited in many Hanafi texts.

Key Terms
الندوة (al-nadwah): Moisture that appears but does not drip when wrung
القياس الخفي (al-qiyas al-khafi): A subtle analogy that requires careful extraction of the effective cause (illa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does a pure garment become impure from contact with a wet impure surface?
A) If the moisture appears and the garment feels damp
B) If the moisture does not drip when wrung but leaves a color trace
C) Only if the moisture drips upon wringing
D) If the moisture is from water and no trace appears

Key Takeaway
Non-dripping moisture (nadwah) from an impure source does not transfer impurity unless it is the impurity itself or leaves a trace, based on a qiyas to the moisture after the third wash.

Critique of Qadikhan and Ruling on Water Bird
Critique and ruling on water bird death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a water bird dying in water and distinguish it from the case where it dies outside.

Core Idea
A water bird that dies in water does not spoil it (Imam); if it dies outside, it spoils by consensus.

This short passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq does two things: it notes that the relied-upon fatwa contradicts Qadikhan's earlier correction regarding the soil-water mixture, and it gives a clear ruling on a water bird that dies in a body of water. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi principle that aquatic animals do not spoil water if they die in their element, but they do if they die outside and then fall in.

Guided Path
1
Identify the critique
The first phrase 'أو عكسه فهو مخالف لتصحيح قاضيخان المتقدم' indicates that the fatwa (giving consideration to the pure element) is opposite to what Qadikhan corrected. Recognize that Qadikhan held the mud in soil-water to be impure.
This critique shows the dynamic nature of tarjih within the Hanafi school.
2
Extract the ruling on water bird
The second part gives the rule: 'طير الماء مات فيه لا يفسده عند الإمام وفي غيره يفسده بالاتفاق'. Parse: if the water bird dies in the water, it does not spoil it according to Abu Hanifa; if it dies outside, it spoils by consensus.
'بالاتفاق' refers to consensus among the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on the latter case.
3
Understand the basis
The distinction rests on whether the animal is an aquatic creature. In their natural habitat, their death does not render the water impure, as they are considered part of the environment. Outside, they are foreign impurities.
This aligns with the general principle that everything aquatic is pure until proven otherwise.
4
Note the fatwa position
The phrase 'وعليه الفتوى' indicates that the relied-upon position for fatwa is the consensus view: the water is spoiled if the bird dies outside and then falls in.
Even though Imam Abu Hanifa's view is mentioned, the fatwa is on the more cautious consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عكسه فهو مخالف لتصحيح قاضيخان المتقدم. وفيها : طير الماء مات فيه لا يفسده عند الإمام وفي غيره يفسده بالاتفاق وعليه الفتوى.
Or its opposite, it is contrary to the correction of Qadikhan that preceded. And in it: A water bird that dies in it does not spoil it according to the Imam, but in other than it, it spoils it by consensus, and upon that is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This short passage contains two parts. First, it notes that the fatwa giving consideration to the pure element in the soil-water mixture (mentioned earlier) contradicts Qadikhan's correction. Qadikhan had declared the correct opinion to be that the mud is impure. Second, it gives a ruling about a water bird that dies in a body of water: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the water remains pure (not spoiled). However, if the bird dies outside the water, it spoils the water by consensus. The fatwa is on the latter position. This illustrates a distinction: aquatic animals that die in water do not make it impure, but if they die elsewhere and fall in, they do.

Key Terms
طير الماء (tayr al-ma'): A water bird that lives in water

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The water is spoiled according to all
B) The water is not spoiled according to Abu Hanifa
C) The water is spoiled only if the bird is large
D) The bird itself becomes impure

Key Takeaway
A water bird that dies in water does not spoil it (Imam's view); if it dies outside, it spoils by consensus, and that is the fatwa.

Rulings on Vomit, Well Water, and Bird Remnants from Al-Mujtaba
Presents disputed rulings on impurity of vomit, well water after a mouse dies, and predatory bird remnants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi rulings on vomit, contaminated well water, predatory bird remnants, and washing water, and IDENTIFY the intra-Hanafi dispute on the first two.

Core Idea
Vomit and contaminated well water have two narrations; predatory bird remnants are makruh, not najis; washing water is ghalizah with decreasing purification counts.

This passage from Al-Mujtaba presents several key rulings on impurity (najasah) that are often tested in fiqh study. You will learn the preferred view (al-asahh) on vomit and well water, the classification of predatory bird remnants, and the unique ruling on washing water where each successive wash purifies less.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three topics
The passage covers vomit, well water contaminated by a dead mouse, predatory bird remnants, and washing water.
These are distinct issues with different rulings.
2
Analyze the dispute on vomit and well water
Note that there are two narrations (riwayatan) within the Hanafi school. The preponderant view (al-rajih) declares them ghalizah (heavy impurity).
The existence of two narrations does not mean both are equally valid; one is chosen as mufta bihi.
3
Examine the ruling on predatory bird remnants
The text explicitly states they are not impure at all (laysa bi najs aslan), but only makruh.
This is a clear ruling with no dispute in this passage.
4
Understand the washing water ruling
Washing water used for impurity is also ghalizah, but the number of washes needed for purification decreases: first wash requires three, second requires two, third requires one.
The classification 'ghalizah' implies that the washing water becomes impure after contact with impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى : وفي نجاسة القيء وماء البئر التي وقعت فيها فأرة وماتت روايتان وسؤر سباع الطير غليظة وغسالة النجاسة في المرات الثلاث غليظة على الأصح وإن كانت الأولى تطهر بالثلاث والثانية بالثنتين والثالثة بالواحدة وفيما عدا الأخيرة نظر بل الراجح التغليظ في القيء وماء البئر المتنجس، وأما سؤر سباع الطير فليس بنجس أصلاً بل هو مكروه.
And in al-Mujtaba: Regarding the impurity of vomit and the well water in which a mouse fell and died, there are two narrations. And the residual water of predatory birds is heavy (ghalizah), and the washing water of impurity in the three washes is heavy according to the correct opinion, even though the first is purified by three, the second by two, and the third by one. And regarding other than the last, there is consideration; rather, the preponderant (view) is heaviness in vomit and impure well water. As for the residual water of predatory birds, it is not impure at all, rather it is makruh.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from Al-Mujtaba deals with several issues of impurity. First, vomit and well water that has been contaminated by a dead mouse: there are two narrations (riwayatan) within the Hanafi school regarding their impurity—some say they are impure, others say not. Second, the residual water (saur) of predatory birds is considered 'ghalizah' (heavy impurity), but later it clarifies that it is not impure but merely makruh (disliked). Third, the water used to wash an impure garment for three times: the correct opinion classifies it as 'ghalizah', even though the first wash garment is purified by three washes, the second by two, and the third by one. The term 'ghalizah' indicates that the washing water is impure, but there is a distinction between the number of washes needed for purification. The passage ends by stating that for vomit and impure well water, the preponderant view is that they are 'ghalizah' (heavy impurity).

Key Terms
غليظة (ghalizah): A category of impurity considered 'heavy' in terms of its ruling
سؤر سباع الطير (saur siba' al-tayr): The residual water (leftover drink) of predatory birds

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Mujtaba, what is the ruling on the residual water (saur) of predatory birds?
A) It is impure (najis)
B) It is makruh (disliked)
C) It is pure and recommended
D) It is purifying

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that vomit and contaminated well water are impure (ghalizah) by preponderant opinion, predatory bird remnants are makruh not najis, and washing water is ghalizah with decreasing purification counts.

Mouse in Vinegar and Clarified Butter: Hanafi Rulings on Impurity
Presents two specific impurity rulings from Umdat al-Fatawa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for a mouse found dead in vinegar (after takhallul) and in clarified butter, distinguishing between solid and liquid forms.

Core Idea
A dead mouse in wine becomes pure when the wine turns to vinegar; in solid butter only the area around it is removed, but in liquid butter the whole becomes impure.

This passage from Umdat al-Fatawa presents two common scenarios that test the Hanafi principles of istihala (transformation) and najasah (impurity) in food substances. The rulings illustrate a key distinction between solid and liquid forms, which determines the extent of contamination and permissible use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two main cases: a mouse dying in wine that later turns to vinegar, and a mouse dying in clarified butter (saman). Note the sub-case of liquid vs solid butter.
2
Apply the principle of istihala to the vinegar case
When the impure wine transforms into vinegar naturally (takhallul), the substance becomes pure (tahir). The mouse's impurity is considered removed by the transformation.
If the transformation was induced (by adding something), there is disagreement; the text says 'in one narration; that is the correct (opinion)'.
3
Distinguish solid vs liquid butter
For solid clarified butter (saman jamid), only the part around the mouse is removed; the rest is edible. For liquid butter, the entire substance becomes impure and cannot be eaten, but it can be used for non-food purposes like lighting and tanning.
The term 'tasharrub' (absorption) is excused in the liquid case for tanning.
4
Note the exception for dead animal fat
Fat from a dead animal (maytah) can be used for lighting but not for tanning leather, unlike the liquid butter case. This shows a distinction between different types of impure animal fats.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عمدة الفتاوى للصدر الشهيد : فأرة ماتت في الخمر وتخللت طاب الخل في رواية هو الصحيح. فأرة ماتت في السمن الجامد يقور ما حولها ويرمى ويؤكل الباقي، فإن كان مائعاً لا يؤكل ويستصبح به ويدبغ به الجلد والتشرب معفو عنه ودك الميتة يستصبح به ولا يدبغ به الجلد ا.هـ.
And in Umdat al-Fatawa by al-Sadr al-Shahid: A mouse that died in wine and then it turned into vinegar, the vinegar is good (pure) in one narration; that is the correct (opinion). A mouse that died in solid clarified butter (saman jamid) – the area around it is dug out and thrown away, and the rest is eaten. If it was liquid, it is not eaten but used for lighting and for tanning leather, and absorption is excused. And the fat of dead animals is used for lighting but not for tanning leather. End.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This lesson covers two cases from Umdat al-Fatawa. First, if a mouse dies in wine and the wine then turns into vinegar (takhallul), the resulting vinegar is considered pure according to the correct narration. This is based on the principle of istihala (transformation) where the impure substance changes into a pure one. Second, if a mouse dies in clarified butter: if the butter is solid (jamid), one removes the area around the mouse and the rest is edible. If the butter is liquid, the entire butter becomes impure and cannot be eaten; however, it can be used for lighting lamps and for tanning leather (since tanning involves non-food use). Absorption (tasharrub) of the impure fat into the skin is excused. The fat of dead animals (deed al-maytah) can be used for lighting but not for tanning leather, unlike the butter case. This distinction reflects different rules for different types of impure substances.

Key Terms
التخلل (al-takhallul): The process of wine turning into vinegar
السمن الجامد (al-saman al-jamid): Solid clarified butter
التشرب (al-tasharrub): Absorption of impure substance into a material
دك الميتة (dukk al-maytah): Fat of a dead animal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Umdat al-Fatawa, if a mouse dies in solid clarified butter, what is the ruling?
A) The entire butter is impure and must be discarded
B) The area around the mouse is removed and the rest is eaten
C) The butter is used only for lighting
D) The butter becomes pure after washing

Key Takeaway
Hanafi taharah rulings on dead animals in food depend on the substance's state: transformation (istihala) purifies wine becoming vinegar, and in solids only the contaminated portion is removed, but liquids become entirely impure yet retain limited functional use.

Finding a Dead Mouse in a Bottle
Ruling on mouse in bottle uncertainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a dead mouse is found in a bottle and its origin is unknown.

Core Idea
Uncertainty about where a mouse died defaults to the smallest container.

When a dead mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and you don't know if it died there, in the larger jar, or in the well, the ruling is to attribute it to the bottle. This prevents assuming impurity of a larger water source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects and the uncertainty
There is a bottle, a jar, and a well. The mouse could have died in any of them, but you only know it is in the bottle now.
2
Apply the principle of attributing to the smaller container
The bottle is the smallest container. The default is to assume the mouse died there, thus not contaminating the jar or well. This is based on the maxim 'al-yaqeen la yazool bi al-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt).
3
State the ruling
The water in the bottle is considered impure, but the jar and well remain pure. You do not assume the worst without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عدة الفتاوى : إذا وجد في القمقمة فأرة ولا يدري أهي فيها ماتت أم في الجرة أم في البئر، تحمل على القمقمة ا هـ. وفي مآل الفتاوى : ماء المطر إذا . على العذرات لا ينجس إلا أن تكون العذرة أكثر من الأرض الطاهرة أو تكون العذرة عند الميزاب. إذا فسا في السراويل وصلى معه قال بعضهم : لا يجوز لأن في الريح أجزاء لطيفة فتدخل أجزاء الثوب . وقيل : إن الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني كان يصلي من غير السراويل ولا تأويل لفعله إلا التحرز من الخلاف،
And in Uddat al-Fatawa: If a mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and it is not known whether it died there or in the jar or in the well, it is attributed to the bottle. End. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater when on dung (al-udhrat) is not impure unless the dung is more than the pure land or the dung is at the spout. If someone passes wind in his trousers and prays with that, some say it is not permissible because the wind contains subtle particles that enter the particles of the garment. And it is said that the imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani used to pray without trousers, and there is no interpretation for his action except caution against the disagreement.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This lesson compiles several rulings from collections of fatwa. First, if a mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and it is uncertain whether it died there, in the larger jar, or in the well, the default is to attribute it to the bottle (i.e., assume it died in the bottle) to avoid assuming contamination of the well or jar. Second, regarding rainwater falling on dung: the water does not become impure unless the dung covers more area than the clean ground, or the dung is directly under the spout (so the water passes over it). This is based on the principle that small amounts of impurity in large amounts of water are excused. Third, a person who passes wind (fasa) in his trousers and then prays without changing: some scholars say it is not permissible because the wind contains fine particles that might transfer impurity to the garment. However, Imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani used to pray without wearing trousers at all, to avoid the dispute entirely. This shows the importance of caution in matters of potential impurity.

Key Terms
القمقمة (al-qumqumah): A bottle or container often used for water

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Uddat al-Fatawa, when a mouse is found in a qumqumah and its origin is unknown, what is the ruling?
A) The jar becomes impure
B) The well becomes impure
C) The mouse is attributed to the qumqumah
D) All containers become impure

Key Takeaway
Uncertainty about the origin of impurity is resolved by confining it to the smallest container.

Rainwater Falling on Dung
Ruling on rainwater falling on dung

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when rainwater on dung becomes impure according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Rainwater on dung is pure unless the dung dominates the area or is at the spout.

When rain falls on dung (al-udhrat), the water does not automatically become impure. It remains pure unless the dung covers more area than the clean ground, or the dung is directly under the spout where water flows over it. This is based on the principle that small amounts of impurity are excused in large amounts of water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios where rainwater becomes impure
Two conditions make the rainwater impure: 1) the dung covers more area than the pure land, or 2) the dung is located at the spout (mizab) where the water falls.
2
Apply the principle of 'al-ʿafw ʿan al-yasīr' (excusing small amounts)
If the dung is small compared to the clean land, the water is considered pure because the impurity is negligible. If the dung is predominant or at the spout, the water becomes impure.
3
State the ruling
In general, rainwater on dung is pure unless one of the two exceptions applies.

Source Text

And in Uddat al-Fatawa: If a mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and it is not known whether it died there or in the jar or in the well, it is attributed to the bottle. End. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater when on dung (al-udhrat) is not impure unless the dung is more than the pure land or the dung is at the spout. If someone passes wind in his trousers and prays with that, some say it is not permissible because the wind contains subtle particles that enter the particles of the garment. And it is said that the imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani used to pray without trousers, and there is no interpretation for his action except caution against the disagreement.

Commentary
This lesson compiles several rulings from collections of fatwa. First, if a mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and it is uncertain whether it died there, in the larger jar, or in the well, the default is to attribute it to the bottle (i.e., assume it died in the bottle) to avoid assuming contamination of the well or jar. Second, regarding rainwater falling on dung: the water does not become impure unless the dung covers more area than the clean ground, or the dung is directly under the spout (so the water passes over it). This is based on the principle that small amounts of impurity in large amounts of water are excused. Key Terms
العذرات (al-udhrat): Dung or feces (plural)
الميزاب (al-mizab): Spout or drainpipe

C) When it rains heavily
D) When the water is used for wudu

Key Takeaway
Rainwater on dung is pure by default; impurity only occurs when the dung is extensive or at the spout.

Passing Wind While Praying and Trousers
Ruling on passing wind in trousers and al-Halwani's practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling regarding praying after passing wind in trousers and the cautionary practice of Imam al-Halwani.

Core Idea
Some scholars deem prayer invalid if wind passes in trousers due to subtle particles; Imam al-Halwani avoided trousers to avoid dispute.

When a person passes wind (fasa) in his trousers and then prays without changing, some scholars say the prayer is not permissible because the wind contains subtle particles that may transfer impurity to the garment. However, Imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani used to pray without trousers altogether to avoid the disagreement entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: passing wind in trousers
Some scholars argue that wind contains fine particles (ajza' latifa) that can transfer impurity to the trousers, making the garment impure.
2
Understand the scholarly difference (ikhtilaf)
There is a disagreement: some say prayer is invalid, while others (implied) consider it valid. Imam al-Halwani's action shows he was cautious.
3
Recognize the principle of caution (ihtiyat)
Imam al-Halwani removed his trousers entirely to avoid any potential impurity, even if the majority view might allow it.
4
Apply the lesson to practice
The preferred view is that the prayer is valid unless there is certainty of impurity, but caution is commendable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عدة الفتاوى : إذا وجد في القمقمة فأرة ولا يدري أهي فيها ماتت أم في الجرة أم في البئر، تحمل على القمقمة ا هـ. وفي مآل الفتاوى : ماء المطر إذا . على العذرات لا ينجس إلا أن تكون العذرة أكثر من الأرض الطاهرة أو تكون العذرة عند الميزاب. إذا فسا في السراويل وصلى معه قال بعضهم : لا يجوز لأن في الريح أجزاء لطيفة فتدخل أجزاء الثوب . وقيل : إن الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني كان يصلي من غير السراويل ولا تأويل لفعله إلا التحرز من الخلاف،
And in Uddat al-Fatawa: If a mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and it is not known whether it died there or in the jar or in the well, it is attributed to the bottle. End. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater when on dung (al-udhrat) is not impure unless the dung is more than the pure land or the dung is at the spout. If someone passes wind in his trousers and prays with that, some say it is not permissible because the wind contains subtle particles that enter the particles of the garment. And it is said that the imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani used to pray without trousers, and there is no interpretation for his action except caution against the disagreement.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This lesson compiles several rulings from collections of fatwa. First, if a mouse is found in a bottle (qumqumah) and it is uncertain whether it died there, in the larger jar, or in the well, the default is to attribute it to the bottle (i.e., assume it died in the bottle) to avoid assuming contamination of the well or jar. Second, regarding rainwater falling on dung: the water does not become impure unless the dung covers more area than the clean ground, or the dung is directly under the spout (so the water passes over it). This is based on the principle that small amounts of impurity in large amounts of water are excused. Third, a person who passes wind (fasa) in his trousers and then prays without changing: some scholars say it is not permissible because the wind contains fine particles that might transfer impurity to the garment. However, Imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani used to pray without wearing trousers at all, to avoid the dispute entirely. This shows the importance of caution in matters of potential impurity.

Key Terms
الفساء (al-fasa'): The passing of wind with a sound (farting)
التحرز من الخلاف (al-taharruz min al-khilaf): Caution against disagreement; taking extra action to avoid a disputed matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam al-Halwani pray without trousers?
A) Because trousers are impure in general
B) To avoid the scholarly disagreement about wind particles
C) Because he had no trousers
D) Because it is sunnah to pray without trousers

Key Takeaway
Prayer after passing wind in trousers is valid in the Hanafi school, but caution (ihtiyat) is recommended to avoid dispute.
