Five Positions on the Purity of Mud in the Hanafi School
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the purity of mud when soil and water mix.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five Hanafi positions on the soil-water mixture ruling, explain the weak basis of the istihala argument as critiqued by Ibn al-Humam, and apply the principle al-natijatu tabi'atun li'akhas al-muqaddimatayn to similar mixtures.

Core Idea
If one component of a mixture is impure, the mixture is impure — the result follows the inferior premise.

In the Hanafi school, the ruling on a mixture of soil and water (mud) when one component is impure is disputed. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq surveys five opinions, critiques the justification of 'becoming a new substance' (istihala), and ultimately affirms the view that the mixture is impure based on the logical principle that the result follows the inferior of the two premises. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze compound substances in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the ruling on a mixture of soil and water (mud) when one of its components (soil or water) is impure? Is the entire mixture considered impure, or does the presence of a pure component render it pure?
2
List the five Hanafi opinions
The passage enumerates five positions: (1) Mud is always impure regardless (Qadikhan, Abu al-Layth, also narrated from Abu Yusuf). (2) Consideration is given to the water — if water is impure, mud is impure; otherwise pure. (3) Consideration is given to the soil. (4) Consideration is given to the majority component. (5) Whichever component is pure, the mud is pure (the view of Muhammad al-Shaybani, Abu Nasr, and some say fatwa).
This diversity exists within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Analyze the justification of istihala
The fifth opinion (mud is pure if any component is pure) is justified by saying the mixture becomes 'something else' (istihala). The author of Al-Bahr al-Raiq critiques this as weak because it would imply that any food or compound with an impure ingredient becomes pure by transformation, which is not accepted.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Apply the principle 'al-natijatu tabi'atun li'akhas al-muqaddimatayn'
The correct view, affirmed by Abu al-Layth and Qadikhan, is that the result follows the inferior of the two premises. If either the soil or water is impure, the mixture is impure. This prevents the absurd consequences of the istihala argument.
This principle is a logical maxim applicable to all mixtures.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: if a person uses mud made with impure water for tayammum or if mud splashes on clothes, the majority view (impure) renders the tayammum invalid and the garments impure, while the minority view might permit it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسراج الوهاج والبزازية وكلهم أطلقوه عن ذكر الخلاف قوله : (فهو مخالف لتصحيح قاضيخان) أقول : قد مشى في المنية على ما ذكره قاضيخان وقال شارحها وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث. وكذا روي عن أبي يوسف ذكره في الخلاصة . وقيل : العبرة للماء إن كان نجساً فالطين نجس وإلا فطاهر. وقيل: العبرة للتراب. وقيل للغالب قال ابن الهمام والأكثر على أنه أيهما كان طاهراً فالطين طاهر اهـ . وهو اختيار أبي نصر محمد بن سلام قال البزازي وهو قول محمد. وقد ذكر أن الفتوى عليه اهـ. ووجهه في الخلاصة بصيرورته شيئاً آخر وهو توجيه ضعيف إذ يقتضي أن جميع الأطعمة إذا كان ماؤها نجساً أو دهنها أو نحو ذلك أن يكون الطعام طاهراً لصيرورته شيئاً آخر، وعلى هذا سائر المركبات إذا كان بعض مفرداتها نجساً ولا يخفى فساده فلله در الفقيه أبي الليث ودر قاضيخان حيث جعل قوله وهو الصحيح مشيراً إلى أن سائر الأقوال لا صحة لها بل هي فاسدة لأن النتيجة تابعة لأخس المقدمتين .اهـ. قوله: (وفيما عدا الأخيرة) أي من المسائل الأربع التي في
And al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Bazzaziyya, and all of them stated it without mentioning the disagreement. His statement: (It is contrary to the correction of Qadikhan that preceded). I say: Al-Muniyya followed what Qadikhan mentioned, and its commentator said it is the choice of al-Faqih Abi al-Layth. Likewise, it is narrated from Abu Yusuf in al-Khulasa. And it was said: Consideration is for the water: if it is impure, the mud is impure; otherwise it is pure. And it was said: Consideration is for the soil. And it was said: For the majority. Ibn al-Humam said: The majority (hold) that whichever is pure, the mud is pure. That is the choice of Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam. Al-Bazzazi said it is the saying of Muhammad. And it has been mentioned that the fatwa is upon it. Its justification in al-Khulasa is that it becomes something else (i.e., a new substance), which is a weak reasoning because it would imply that all foods, if their water or oil is impure, would be pure because they become something else. And similarly all compounds if some of their components are impure, its corruption is not hidden. So blessed is al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and Qadikhan, who made his statement the correct one, indicating that other opinions have no soundness, rather they are corrupt, because the result follows the inferior of the two premises. End. His statement: (And other than the last) i.e., from the four issues that are in...
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute over the soil-water mixture ruling. It begins by noting that some texts like al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Bazzaziyya mention the fatwa without noting the disagreement, but the author criticizes this as contrary to Qadikhan's correction. The author then surveys multiple opinions: (1) the opinion of Qadikhan and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, also narrated from Abu Yusuf, that the mud is impure regardless; (2) an opinion giving consideration to the water—if it is impure, the mud is impure; (3) consideration to the soil; (4) consideration to the majority; (5) the view of Ibn al-Humam and the majority that whichever is pure, the mud is pure, which is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Nasr, and upon which some say the fatwa rests. However, the author (likely Ibn al-Humam) criticizes the justification that the mixture becomes a new substance (istihala), arguing that this would absurdly imply that any food with an impure ingredient becomes pure by transformation. He praises Abu al-Layth and Qadikhan for insisting on the correct view that the mud is impure, citing the principle that the result follows the inferior premise (i.e., if any input is impure, the mixture is impure). The final sentence 'and other than the last' refers to a list of four issues that is cut off.

Key Terms
النتيجة تابعة لأخس المقدمتين (al-natijatu tabi'atun li'akhas al-muqaddimatayn): The result follows the inferior of the two premises — in fiqh, if any component of a mixture is impure, the mixture is impure.
استحالة (istihala): Transformation into a new substance, argued to change the ruling of the original components.
الطين (al-teen): Mud, a mixture of soil and water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mud is not actually a new substance
B) It would imply that all foods and compounds become pure when mixed with an impure ingredient, which is not accepted
C) The soil is always impure
D) The water is always impure

Key Takeaway
The result follows the inferior of the two premises: if any part of a mixture is impure, the mixture is impure.

Water Bird Dying in Water: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on water bird dying in water, with difference between Abu Hanifa and others

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi position on a water bird dying in water and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
A water bird's carcass in water renders it impure according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, despite Abu Hanifa's dissenting view.

This brief passage from *al-Bahr al-Rāʼiq* highlights an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding purity of water when a water bird (like a duck or goose) dies in it. Although Abu Hanifa maintained the water remains pure, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that it becomes impure, and the fatwa is based on their view. Understanding this case clarifies how the Hanafi school resolves disagreements between its founding imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what act is being ruled upon: a water bird dies in a body of water. The ruling concerns whether the water remains pure (ṭāhir) or becomes impure (najis).
The passage does not specify the size of the water body; later Hanafi texts may add conditions like the amount of water (e.g., less than a well).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to the text, Abu Hanifa (al-Imām) holds that the bird's death does not spoil the water. This is his individual view, not shared by his main disciples.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning may be that water birds are inherently connected to water, so their carcasses are not considered impure unless they cause a change in the water's properties.
3
State the position of the two companions (Sāḥibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the water becomes impure. The text says 'according to others it spoils it by agreement', meaning the Sāḥibayn are unanimous on this point.
This position is more cautious (iḥtiyāṭ) and aligns with the general rule that any carcass in water renders it impure.
4
Determine the muftā bihī (operative ruling)
The text explicitly states 'and the fatwa is upon it' (wa ʿalayhi al-fatwā), meaning the relied-upon view for issuing fatwas is that of the Sāḥibayn. This is also the position transmitted in the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.
Thus, despite Abu Hanifa's stature, his solitary opinion is not given precedence over the majority of the school.
5
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
If a water bird dies in a well, according to the fatwa the water is impure and cannot be used for purification (e.g., wuḍūʾ) unless the carcass is removed and the water is scooped out or treated. According to Abu Hanifa, the water could be used as is.
This difference affects daily practice, especially in rural settings where wells are common.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها : طير الماء مات فيه لا يفسده عند الإمام وفي غيره يفسده بالاتفاق وعليه الفتوى.
And within it: A water bird that dies in it does not spoil it according to the Imam, and according to others it spoils it by agreement, and the fatwa is upon it.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a water bird that dies in a body of water. According to Abu Hanifa (the Imam), the dead bird does not render the water impure (najis) as long as it did not die from the water itself. However, according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the water becomes impure due to the carcass. The relied-upon position for fatwa is the view of the companions, which is the majority position. The phrase 'by agreement' indicates that the companions agreed on the impurity, while Abu Hanifa held a dissenting opinion.

Key Terms
طير الماء (tayr al-maa): A water bird, e.g., duck, goose, that lives in water.
عند الإمام (inda al-imam): Refers to Abu Hanifa, eponym of the Hanafi school.
في غيره (fi ghayrihi): In the view of others besides Abu Hanifa, i.e., the two companions (Sahibayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The water remains pure (Abu Hanifa's view).
B) The water becomes impure (Sahibayn's view).
C) The water becomes impure only if the bird was dead for more than a day.
D) The bird's death does not affect the water at all.

Key Takeaway
Although Abu Hanifa holds that a water bird's carcass does not spoil water, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that it does, according to the two companions.

Wash Water of the Deceased: Impurity or Usage?
Ruling on ghusala from Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on ghusalat al-mayyit and identify the condition under which it is not impure.

Core Idea
Wash water of the deceased is impure only if the body had physical impurity; otherwise it is merely used water.

When washing a deceased person, the water used (ghusala) may be considered impure. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the deceased's body carried any physical impurity. In this lesson, we examine the difference between Imam Muhammad's general ruling and the sounder opinion that takes into account the condition of the body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
State the act (ghusala of the deceased) and the initial ruling given by Imam Muhammad in Al-Asl.
2
Recognize the condition that changes the ruling
Understand the condition 'if there is no impurity on the body' which alters the ruling from impure to musta'mal (not impure).
3
Understand the basis for Imam Muhammad's general statement
Note that Imam Muhammad's unconditional ruling is based on the typical presence of impurity on the deceased's body, not on absolute principle.
This is a key distinction between the literal text and the correct legal view.
4
Apply the correct ruling to a scenario
Given a scenario where the deceased was known to be clean (e.g., washed from najas before death), state the ruling.
Practice using the correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراج الوهاج : غسالة الميت نجسة، أطلق ذلك محمد في الأصل والأصح أنه إذا لم يكن على بدنه نجاسة يصير الماء مستعملاً ولا يكون نجساً إلا أن محمداً إنما أطلق ذلك لأن بدن الميت لا يخلو عن نجاسة غالباً، ودخان النجاسة إذا أصاب الثوب أو البدن فيه اختلاف والصحيح أنه لا ينجسه بيض ما لا يؤكل لحمه إذا انكسر على ثوب إنسان فأصابه من ومحه فيه اختلاف؛ منهم من قال إنه نجس اعتباراً بلحم ما لا يؤكل ولبنه لأنه محرم الأكل، وقيل هو طاهر اعتباراً ببيض الدجاجة الميتة ا هـ .
And in Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The wash water of the deceased is impure. Muhammad stated this in Al-Asl, but the correct view is that if there is no impurity on his body, the water becomes used (musta'mal) and is not impure; however, Muhammad only stated that because the body of the deceased is rarely free from impurity. And regarding the smoke of impurity if it strikes clothing or the body, there is a difference, and the correct view is that it does not make it impure. The egg of a non-edible bird, if it breaks on a person's clothing and gets onto it, there is a difference: some say it is impure considering the meat and milk of the non-edible animal because its consumption is prohibited; others say it is pure considering the egg of a dead chicken. End quote.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj presents three distinct rulings. First, the wash water (ghusala) used to wash a deceased person is usually impure, as stated by Imam Muhammad in Al-Asl. However, the sounder opinion is that if the deceased's body was clean (free from physical impurities like najasah), the water becomes musta'mal (used) and is not impure; but Muhammad's unconditional statement is based on the fact that a deceased body is generally not free from impurity. Second, the smoke arising from a source of impurity (e.g., burning najasah) does not render the clothing or body impure according to the correct view, despite some difference. Third, the egg of a bird whose meat is not eaten (e.g., birds of prey) is disputed: some scholars deem it impure by analogy to its non-edible meat and milk, while others deem it pure by analogy to the egg of a dead chicken (which is pure if the egg's shell was intact). The text does not specify which view is preponderant here.

Key Terms
غسالة الميت (ghusalat al-mayyit): The water used to wash the deceased as part of ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always impure regardless of body condition
B) Impure only if the body had physical impurity
C) Always pure because it is musta'mal
D) Makruh but not najis

Key Takeaway
The ghusala ruling is conditional: impure if the body has najas, otherwise musta'mal.

Smoke of Impurity: Does It Transfer Impurity?
Ruling on smoke from impurity from Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on smoke produced from an impure substance and explain why it does not cause impurity.

Core Idea
Smoke from burning an impure substance does not transmit impurity to clothing or body.

When an impure substance burns, the resulting smoke may come into contact with clothing or skin. Is that smoke considered impure? The Hanafi school addresses this question with a clear ruling supported by a difference of opinion and a preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
Consider the case: smoke from an impure source (e.g., burning najasah) hitting a person's clothing or body.
2
Recognize the difference of opinion
Note that there is ikhtilaf (difference) among scholars on this ruling.
3
Establish the correct view
The text declares that the correct view (al-sahih) is that the smoke does not make the clothing or body impure.
The ruling is clearly stated: 'لا ينجسه'.

Egg of Non-Edible Bird: Impure or Pure?
Ruling on egg of non-edible bird from Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on the egg of a non-edible bird and identify the basis for each analogy.

Core Idea
The egg of a non-edible bird is disputed: some deem it impure by analogy to its meat, others pure by analogy to a dead chicken's egg.

If an egg from a bird whose meat is not permissible (e.g., a bird of prey) breaks on your clothing, is it impure? The Hanafi scholars differ on this question, offering two analogies: one comparing it to the non-edible meat and milk, and another comparing it to the egg of a dead chicken (which is pure if the shell is intact). This lesson explores both views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and its relevance
Consider the case of an egg from a non-edible bird breaking on clothing. The question is whether that egg is impure.
2
State the first opinion and its basis
Some scholars say it is impure (najis) by analogy to the meat and milk of such animals, which are prohibited and considered impure.
Analogy (qiyas) to the meat and milk.
3
State the second opinion and its basis
Others say it is pure (tahir) by analogy to the egg of a dead chicken, which is pure as long as the shell is intact.
Analogy to the egg of a chicken that died (maytah).
4
Note the lack of a preferred view
The text does not explicitly declare which opinion is correct, leaving the issue open for further tarjih.
This is an example of an unresolved ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراج الوهاج : غسالة الميت نجسة، أطلق ذلك محمد في الأصل والأصح أنه إذا لم يكن على بدنه نجاسة يصير الماء مستعملاً ولا يكون نجساً إلا أن محمداً إنما أطلق ذلك لأن بدن الميت لا يخلو عن نجاسة غالباً، ودخان النجاسة إذا أصاب الثوب أو البدن فيه اختلاف والصحيح أنه لا ينجسه بيض ما لا يؤكل لحمه إذا انكسر على ثوب إنسان فأصابه من ومحه فيه اختلاف؛ منهم من قال إنه نجس اعتباراً بلحم ما لا يؤكل ولبنه لأنه محرم الأكل، وقيل هو طاهر اعتباراً ببيض الدجاجة الميتة ا هـ .
And in Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The wash water of the deceased is impure. Muhammad stated this in Al-Asl, but the correct view is that if there is no impurity on his body, the water becomes used (musta'mal) and is not impure; however, Muhammad only stated that because the body of the deceased is rarely free from impurity. And regarding the smoke of impurity if it strikes clothing or the body, there is a difference, and the correct view is that it does not make it impure. The egg of a non-edible bird, if it breaks on a person's clothing and gets onto it, there is a difference: some say it is impure considering the meat and milk of the non-edible animal because its consumption is prohibited; others say it is pure considering the egg of a dead chicken. End quote.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj presents three distinct rulings. First, the wash water (ghusala) used to wash a deceased person is usually impure, as stated by Imam Muhammad in Al-Asl. However, the sounder opinion is that if the deceased's body was clean (free from physical impurities like najasah), the water becomes musta'mal (used) and is not impure; but Muhammad's unconditional statement is based on the fact that a deceased body is generally not free from impurity. Second, the smoke arising from a source of impurity (e.g., burning najasah) does not render the clothing or body impure according to the correct view, despite some difference. Third, the egg of a bird whose meat is not eaten (e.g., birds of prey) is disputed: some scholars deem it impure by analogy to its non-edible meat and milk, while others deem it pure by analogy to the egg of a dead chicken (which is pure if the egg's shell was intact). The text does not specify which view is preponderant here.

Key Terms
بيض ما لا يؤكل لحمه (bayd ma la yu'akalu lahmuhu): Egg of a bird whose meat is not permissible to eat (e.g., birds of prey).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, what is the basis for the opinion that the egg of a non-edible bird is pure?
A) Analogy to the milk of a non-edible animal
B) Analogy to the egg of a dead chicken
C) The egg is not a part of the bird's body
D) The egg is from a living creature

Key Takeaway
The egg of a non-edible bird is subject to ikhtilaf: najis by analogy to its meat, tahir by analogy to a dead chicken's egg.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Impurity Cases: Vomit, Well Water, Su'r, and Wash Water
Four rulings on impurities with internal Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi ruling for each of the four impurity cases and explain why taghliz is preponderant for vomit and well water while su'r of predatory birds is only makruh.

Core Idea
Two narrations exist for vomit and well water; taghliz is preponderant, but predatory bird su'r is not impure at all, only makruh.

This passage from Al-Mujtaba presents four distinct rulings on purity matters, each with internal Hanafi differences. You will learn to navigate the riwayatan (two narrations) and understand how the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) weighed the evidence to arrive at the mufta bihi (operative) positions. The key is distinguishing between impurity (najasah) and disliked but not impure (makruh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four cases
List the four scenarios: (1) vomit, (2) well water contaminated by a dead mouse, (3) the residual water (su'r) of predatory birds, and (4) the wash water (ghusalat) of an impure object after three washes.
These are independent rulings, but some share the same category (two narrations).
2
Note the two narrations (riwayatan) for case 1 and 2
Understand that for vomit and well water, there is an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf: one narration may treat them as impure (najas) with taghliz, another as less severe. The preponderant (rajih) view is taghliz (severe impurity).
The text does not specify which imam transmitted each narration, only that two exist.
3
Determine the ruling for su'r of predatory birds (case 3)
The text explicitly states that the su'r of predatory birds is NOT impure at all (laysa bi najisin aslan); rather, it is makruh (disliked). This is a clear exception.
This contrasts with the su'r of dogs and swine, which is najis.
4
Analyze the wash water (ghusalat al-najasah) ruling (case 4)
The text says that on the correct view (al-ashah), the wash water after three washes is ghalizah (severe impurity). Even though the object becomes pure after the third wash, the used wash water itself is considered impure. The phrase about first, second, third refers to the purification of the object, not the water.
This ruling is about the water that has been used to wash an impure item; it is considered contaminated.
5
Synthesize the mufta bihi positions
Combine all: vomit and well water are treated as severe impurity (taghliz); su'r of predatory birds is makruh, not najis; and ghusalat al-najasah after three washes is also severe impurity. This is the relied-upon view in the school.
The tarjih authority is the statement 'wa al-rajih al-taghliz' and 'f-aṣ-ṣaḥḥ' for the wash water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى : وفي نجاسة القيء وماء البئر التي وقعت فيها فأرة وماتت روايتان وسؤر سباع الطير غليظة وغسالة النجاسة في المرات الثلاث غليظة على الأصح وإن كانت الأولى تطهر بالثلاث والثانية بالثنتين والثالثة بالواحدة وفيما عدا الأخيرة نظر بل الراجح التغليظ في القيء وماء البئر المتنجس، وأما سؤر سباع الطير فليس بنجس أصلاً بل هو مكروه.
And in Al-Mujtaba: Regarding the impurity of vomit and the water of a well in which a mouse fell and died, there are two narrations. And the residual water (su'r) of predatory birds is serious (ghalizah), and the wash water of impurity in three washes is serious (ghalizah) according to the correct view, even though the first becomes pure by three, the second by two, and the third by one. But regarding other than the last [case], there is consideration, rather the preponderant is the severity (taghliz) concerning vomit and the contaminated well water. As for the su'r of predatory birds, it is not impure at all; rather it is makruh.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from Al-Mujtaba discusses four issues. First, the impurity of vomit and well water contaminated by a mouse's death: there are two narrations (riwayatan) within the Hanafi school regarding their impurity level—some indicate they are impure (najis) while others may treat them differently. Second, the residual water (su'r) left after predatory birds (like hawks) drink is considered serious impurity (ghalizah) in the context of being avoided, but the text later clarifies that this su'r is not actually impure (najis) but only makruh (disliked). Third, the water used to wash off impurity (ghusalat al-najasah) after three washes is also considered serious impurity on the correct view, meaning that the wash water from each wash is impure, even though the object being washed becomes pure after the third wash. The first wash water is pure after three rinses, second after two, third after one? Actually, the text says 'the first becomes pure by three, the second by two, the third by one'—this refers to the washing of the object: after three washes the object is pure, and the second wash makes it pure after two additional washes? The phrasing is ambiguous. The overall point is that the preponderant view (al-rajih) is to apply strictness (taghliz) regarding vomit and contaminated well water, meaning they should be treated as serious impurities. However, the su'r of predatory birds is a clear exception: it is not impure at all, only makruh.

Key Terms
سؤر سباع الطير (su'r siba' al-tayr): The residual water remaining after predatory birds drink from a vessel.
روایتان (riwayatan): Two narrations or transmissions from the founding imams within the Hanafi school.
تغلیظ (taghliz): Severity in ruling, meaning the impurity is considered serious (ghalizah) requiring more rigorous purification.
غسالة النجاسة (ghusalat al-najasah): The water that has been used to wash an impure object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Vomit
B) Well water in which a mouse died
C) The residual water (su'r) of predatory birds
D) The wash water of an impure object after three washes

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, vomit and well water contaminated by a dead mouse are treated as severe impurity (taghliz), while the su'r of predatory birds is only makruh, not impure, and the wash water after washing an impure object is also considered severe impurity.

Mouse in Wine Turning to Vinegar: Purification Through Natural Change
Ruling on mouse in wine that becomes vinegar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a mouse dies in wine that later naturally turns to vinegar and explain the basis for considering the vinegar pure and permissible.

Core Idea
Natural transformation (istihalah) from najis to tahir renders the result pure.

Wine is impure and haram, but what if it turns to vinegar naturally? The Hanafi school considers that the impurity is lifted by natural transformation (istihalah), even if an impurity like a mouse died in it. This passage from 'Umdat al-Fatawa confirms that the resulting vinegar is good and permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A mouse dies in wine, and then the wine naturally turns into vinegar.
The transformation is key.
2
Note the ruling
The vinegar becomes good (halal) and pure.
Text says 'طاب الخل'.
3
Recognize the principle
Natural istihalah purifies the substance.
Even the mouse's impurity is transformed.
4
Understand the ikhtilaf
There is a different narration, but the correct view is permissibility.
Text says 'في رواية هو الصحيح'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عمدة الفتاوى للصدر الشهيد : فأرة ماتت في الخمر وتخللت طاب الخل في رواية هو الصحيح. فأرة ماتت في السمن الجامد يقور ما حولها ويرمى ويؤكل الباقي، فإن كان مائعاً لا يؤكل ويستصبح به ويدبغ به الجلد والتشرب معفو عنه ودك الميتة يستصبح به ولا يدبغ به الجلد ا.هـ.
And in 'Umdat al-Fatawa by al-Sadr al-Shahid: A mouse that died in wine and then the wine turned into vinegar, the vinegar becomes good (permissible) in one narration, and that is the correct view. A mouse that died in solid ghee: dig around it, throw it away, and eat the rest. If it was liquid, do not eat it; use it for lighting (lamp) and use it for tanning leather, and its seepage is excused. The fat (grease) of a dead animal is used for lighting but not used for tanning leather. End quote.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Fatawa presents several practical rulings. First, if a mouse dies in wine (khamr) and the wine subsequently turns into vinegar naturally, the vinegar is pure and permissible to consume; this is one narration and the correct view. Second, if a mouse dies in solid ghee (samn jamid), one may dig around the area where the mouse died, remove that portion, and the rest of the ghee is permissible to eat. However, if the ghee was liquid (sa'il), the entire ghee becomes impure and cannot be eaten; it may only be used for lighting lamps (istishbah) and for tanning leather (dibagh), and the seepage (tasharub) of the impurity is forgiven. Third, the oil (dukhn, rendered fat) of a dead animal (maytah) can be used for lighting but not for tanning leather, because tanning requires a pure substance and the oil of a dead animal is impure.

Key Terms
تخللت (takhallalat): The wine turned into vinegar by itself, naturally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on vinegar that results from wine in which a mouse died?
A) Impure and cannot be used.
B) Good (halal) and pure.
C) Permissible only if the mouse is removed first.
D) Disliked but allowed.

Key Takeaway
Natural istihalah (transformation) can render a najis substance pure, even if an impurity like a dead mouse was present, provided the transformation is complete and natural.

Mouse in Ghee: Solid Versus Liquid Rulings
Ruling on mouse in ghee depending on solid or liquid state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling for a mouse dying in solid ghee versus liquid ghee and state the permissible uses for impure liquid ghee.

Core Idea
In solid ghee, the impurity is localized; in liquid ghee, the entire substance becomes impure.

Ghee is a common cooking fat. If a mouse dies in it, the ruling depends on whether the ghee was solid or liquid at the time. Solid ghee allows removal of the affected portion, while liquid ghee becomes entirely impure but can still be used for non-edible purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the states
Consider whether the ghee is solid (جامد) or liquid (مائع) when the mouse is found.
The state matters.
2
Solid ghee ruling
Dig around the mouse area, discard that portion, and eat the rest.
The impurity is localized.
3
Liquid ghee ruling
The entire ghee becomes impure; do not eat it.
Impurity spreads throughout.
4
Permissible uses for liquid ghee
It can be used for lighting lamps and for tanning leather.
Seepage is excused.

Using Oil from a Dead Animal: Permissible for Lamps but Not Tanning
Ruling on dead animal oil: allowed for lamp, not for tanning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on using oil extracted from a dead animal (maytah) and differentiate between its use for lighting and tanning.

Core Idea
Oil of a dead animal is impure but can be used for lighting, not tanning.

The oil (dukk) of a dead animal is considered najis because the animal itself was not slaughtered properly. However, Hanafi law permits its use for non-edible purposes like lighting lamps, but not for tanning leather because tanning requires a pure substance that can purify the skin.

Guided Path
1
Note the source
Oil from a dead animal (maytah) – an animal that died without proper slaughter.
It is impure (najis).
2
Ruling for lighting
It is permitted to use it for lighting lamps.
Text: 'يستصبح به'.
3
Ruling for tanning
It is not permitted to use it for tanning leather.
Text: 'لا يدبغ به الجلد'.
4
Reason for the difference
Tanning requires a pure substance to purify the skin; oil from dead animal is impure, so ineffective. Lighting tolerates impurity because the oil is burned and not directly contacted.
Hanafi principle: need for purity in dibagh.

Attribution of Impurity in Case of Doubt: The Mouse in the Flask
Fatwa ruling on attribution of impurity when location of death is uncertain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that uncertainty about the source of impurity is resolved by attributing it to the immediate container where the object is found.

Core Idea
Uncertainty about where a dead mouse died is resolved by attributing impurity to the flask where it is found.

This fatwa from 'Iddat al-Fatawa provides a clear example of the Hanafi principle that certainty overcomes doubt. When a dead mouse is discovered in a flask, and there is uncertainty whether it died there, in the water jar, or in the well, the impurity is attributed to the flask itself. The reasoning is that the mouse's presence in the flask is the immediate and observable fact, so it is taken as the default location of death.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Imagine a flask (qumquma) in which a dead mouse is found. The person is uncertain whether the mouse died in the flask, in the larger water jar (jarrah), or in the well. Determine the legal issue: where is the impurity (najasah) attributed?
The flask is the container where the mouse is discovered.
2
Identify the uncertainty
The doubt is only about the location of death. There is no doubt that the mouse is dead and that it is an impure animal (such as a mouse) according to Hanafi rulings.
Uncertainty about the origin of impurity is common in fiqh.
3
Note the resolution principle
The Hanafi fatwa applies the principle of 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt). Since the mouse is found in the flask, that is the certain fact; the doubt about the other locations does not overturn the attribution to the flask.
This is a classic application of the maxim.
4
Apply to the scenario
Thus the flask (qumquma) is considered impure and its contents (liquid) become impure. The water in the jar and the well remain pure unless other evidence arises.
The ruling is practical: clean the flask and discard its contents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عدة الفتاوى : إذا وجد في القمقمة فأرة ولا يدري أهي فيها ماتت أم في الجرة أم في البئر، تحمل على القمقمة ا هـ. وفي مآل الفتاوى : ماء المطر إذا . على العذرات لا ينجس إلا أن تكون العذرة أكثر من الأرض الطاهرة أو تكون العذرة عند الميزاب.
And in 'Iddat al-Fatawa: If a mouse is found in a flask (qumquma) and it is not known whether it died in it, in the pitcher, or in the well, it is attributed to the flask. End quote. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater if it falls on human waste, it does not become impure unless the waste is more than the pure earth, or the waste is at the drainpipe.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage contains two fatwa rulings from different collections. First, from 'Iddat al-Fatawa: If a dead mouse is discovered in a flask (qumquma) and there is uncertainty whether it died in the flask, the large water jar (jarrah), or the well, the impurity is attributed to the flask itself. This is based on the principle that certainty overcomes doubt; since the mouse is found in the flask, it is presumed to have died there. Second, from Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater that falls on human waste (adhrat, dry piles) does not become impure (najis) by mere contact unless the waste is greater in quantity than the surrounding pure earth, or if the waste is directly at the outlet of a drainpipe (mizab) where the water flows over it. The principle is that the water must be overcome by the impurity in quantity or direct flow to become impure.

Key Terms
قمقمة (qumquma): A small flask, often made of glass or pottery, for storing liquids.
الجرة (al-jarrah): A large water jar or pitcher for storing water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa in 'Iddat al-Fatawa, if a dead mouse is found in a flask and you don't know where it died, where is the impurity attributed?
A) To the well
B) To the larger jar
C) To the flask itself
D) To all three containers

Key Takeaway
Uncertainty about the source of impurity is resolved by attributing it to the immediate container where the object of impurity is found.

When Does Rainwater Become Impure? Conditions from Ma'al al-Fatawa
Fatwa ruling on when rainwater becomes impure from solid human waste

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions under which rainwater falling on solid human waste becomes impure in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Rainwater on waste remains pure unless the waste covers more area than the pure earth, or flows directly from a drainpipe.

This fatwa from Ma'al al-Fatawa clarifies that mere contact with impurity does not render rainwater impure. The default ruling is that the water remains pure (tahir). It becomes impure (najis) only if either the waste covers a larger area than the surrounding purified earth, or if the waste is located at the mouth of a drainpipe (mizab) so that the flowing water directly passes over it. These conditions reflect the Hanafi principle that for water to become impure, the impurity must overcome the water in quantity or direct flow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Rainwater that falls on human waste (dry or solid feces) is presumed pure. The default is that the water does not become impure simply by falling on impurity.
This is based on the principle that water is pure unless a change is detected.
2
List the two exceptions
The water becomes impure in two cases: (1) when the waste (adhurat) is greater in area than the surrounding pure earth; (2) when the waste is located directly at the opening of a drainpipe (mizab).
These are not conditions for purity, but conditions that trigger impurity.
3
Understand the logic
In the first case, the waste dominates the area, so the water cannot avoid contact. In the second, the concentrated flow of water from the drainpipe directly hits the waste, making the water carry the impurity.
Hanafis consider the relative quantity and force of water vs impurity.
4
Apply to variations
If a small piece of waste is on the ground and rain falls on it, the water remains pure unless the waste is spread over a larger area or is directly under a downspout.
Similarly, if the waste is on a slope and water runs over it, the ruling may differ if it meets the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي عدة الفتاوى : إذا وجد في القمقمة فأرة ولا يدري أهي فيها ماتت أم في الجرة أم في البئر، تحمل على القمقمة ا هـ. وفي مآل الفتاوى : ماء المطر إذا . على العذرات لا ينجس إلا أن تكون العذرة أكثر من الأرض الطاهرة أو تكون العذرة عند الميزاب.
And in 'Iddat al-Fatawa: If a mouse is found in a flask (qumquma) and it is not known whether it died in it, in the pitcher, or in the well, it is attributed to the flask. End quote. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater if it falls on human waste, it does not become impure unless the waste is more than the pure earth, or the waste is at the drainpipe.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage contains two fatwa rulings from different collections. First, from 'Iddat al-Fatawa: If a dead mouse is discovered in a flask (qumquma) and there is uncertainty whether it died in the flask, the large water jar (jarrah), or the well, the impurity is attributed to the flask itself. This is based on the principle that certainty overcomes doubt; since the mouse is found in the flask, it is presumed to have died there. Second, from Ma'al al-Fatawa: Rainwater that falls on human waste (adhrat, dry piles) does not become impure (najis) by mere contact unless the waste is greater in quantity than the surrounding pure earth, or if the waste is directly at the outlet of a drainpipe (mizab) where the water flows over it. The principle is that the water must be overcome by the impurity in quantity or direct flow to become impure.

Key Terms
العذرات (al-adhurat): Human waste (feces), specifically dry or solid excrement.
الميزاب (al-mizab): A drainpipe or gutter that channels rainwater from the roof.
أكثر من الأرض الطاهرة (akthar min al-ard al-tahira): Greater in area than the surrounding pure earth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa in Ma'al al-Fatawa, when does rainwater that falls on human waste become impure?
A) Always
B) Only if the waste is more than the surrounding pure earth
C) Only if the waste is at the drainpipe
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Rainwater remains pure upon contacting solid waste unless the waste covers a larger area or is directly under a drainpipe.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Praying with Wind in Trousers and Related Impurities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying with wind in trousers and identify the four additional impurities for which the same permissibility applies.

Core Idea
Passing wind does not transfer impurity to clothing; prayer remains valid.

This lesson addresses a practical concern: does passing wind while wearing trousers make the clothing impure and invalidate prayer? The text discusses differences of opinion and concludes with the relied-upon fatwa of permissibility, also extending to certain other minor impurities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disagreement
What is the act being ruled upon? Some scholars said it is not permissible. Why? Because the wind contains fine particles that enter the fabric. Note the reasoning.
2
Understand the Hanafi preference
The text mentions that al-Halwani prayed without trousers to avoid disagreement, and that the fatwa is permissibility. It also lists four other issues (bird not eaten, fish blood, saliva of mule/donkey, urine splashes) where the same ruling applies.
The four issues are mentioned in parentheses, indicating they are additional cases where impurity is overlooked.
3
Apply the ruling
State the final fatwa: it is permissible whether the trousers were wet or dry at the time of passing wind. Identify the common condition: the impurity must be less than a dirham in size.
The text says 'الفتوى أنه يجوز' without restriction to size, but from context it is understood to apply to minor impurities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا فسا في السراويل وصلى معه قال بعضهم : لا يجوز لأن في الريح أجزاء لطيفة فتدخل أجزاء الثوب . وقيل : إن الشيخ الإمام شمس الأئمة الحلواني كان يصلي من غير السراويل ولا تأويل لفعله إلا التحرز من الخلاف، والسراج الوهاج والبزازية وكلهم أطلقوه عن ذكر الخلاف قوله : (فهو مخالف لتصحيح قاضيخان) أقول : قد مشى في المنية على ما ذكره قاضيخان وقال شارحها وهو اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث. وكذا روي عن أبي يوسف ذكره في الخلاصة . وقيل : العبرة للماء إن كان نجساً فالطين نجس وإلا فطاهر. وقيل: العبرة للتراب. وقيل للغالب قال ابن الهمام والأكثر على أنه أيهما كان طاهراً فالطين طاهر اهـ . وهو اختيار أبي نصر محمد بن سلام قال البزازي وهو قول محمد. وقد ذكر أن الفتوى عليه اهـ. ووجهه في الخلاصة بصيرورته شيئاً آخر وهو توجيه ضعيف إذ يقتضي أن جميع الأطعمة إذا كان ماؤها نجساً أو دهنها أو نحو ذلك أن يكون الطعام طاهراً لصيرورته شيئاً آخر، وعلى هذا سائر المركبات إذا كان بعض مفرداتها نجساً ولا يخفى فساده فلله در الفقيه أبي الليث ودر قاضيخان حيث جعل قوله وهو الصحيح مشيراً إلى أن سائر الأقوال لا صحة لها بل هي فاسدة لأن النتيجة تابعة لأخس المقدمتين .اهـ. قوله: (وفيما عدا الأخيرة) أي من المسائل الأربع التي في طير لا يؤكل ودم السمك ولعاب البغل والحمار وبول انتضح كرؤوس الإبر والنجس والفتوى أنه يجوز سواء كان السراويل رطباً وقت الفسوة أو يابساً. إذا رأى على ثوب غيره نجاسة أكثر من قدر الدرهم يخبره ولا يسعه تركه جلد مرارة الغنم نجس ومرارته وبوله سواء عند محمد طاهر، وعندهما نجس. ومثانة الغنم حكمه حكم بوله حتى لا تجوز الصلاة معه. إذا زاد على قدر الدرهم قطرة خمر وقعت في دن خل لا يحل شربه إلا بعد ساعة، ولو صب كوز من خمر في دن من خل ولا يوجد له طعم ولا رائحة حل الشراب في الحال. السلق والسلجم المطبوع في رماد العذرة نجس عند أبي يوسف اهـ.
If one passes wind (fasa) in the trousers and prays with it, some said it is not permissible because the wind contains fine particles that enter the fabric of the garment. And it is said that the Shaykh al-Imam Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani would pray without trousers, and there is no explanation for his action except avoidance of disagreement. And Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and Al-Bazzaziyyah, and all of them, stated it without mentioning disagreement: 'So it contradicts the verification of Qadikhan.' I say: In Al-Munya they followed what Qadikhan said, and its commentator said it is the choice of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. And likewise it is narrated from Abu Yusuf in Al-Khulasah. And it is said the consideration is for the water: if the water is impure, then the mud is impure; otherwise it is pure. And it is said the consideration is for the soil. And it is said for the dominant. Ibn al-Humam said the majority are that whichever of them is pure, the mud is pure. End quote. And it is the choice of Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam, and al-Bazzazi said it is the opinion of Muhammad. And it has been mentioned that the fatwa is upon it. End quote. And its justification in Al-Khulasah is that it becomes something else, but that is a weak reasoning because it would imply that all foods, if their water or oil etc. is impure, the food would be pure because it became something else, and likewise all compounds if some of their components are impure - and its corruption is not hidden. So excellent is Abu al-Layth and Qadikhan in making their saying 'and it is the correct' indicating that other statements have no validity, rather they are corrupt because the result follows from the worst of the two premises. End quote. His saying: (and other than the last) i.e., from the four issues: a bird not eaten, fish blood, saliva of mule and donkey, urine that splashed like needle tips, and impurity, and the fatwa is that it is permissible whether the trousers were wet at the time of the wind or dry. If he sees on another's garment impurity more than the size of a dirham, he informs him and it is not permissible for him to leave it. The skin of the sheep's gall bladder is impure, and its gall and urine are equal: according to Muhammad they are pure, according to the two companions they are impure. And the sheep's bladder: its ruling is the same as its urine, so that prayer is not allowed with it. If a drop of wine falls into a jar of vinegar, it is not permissible to drink it until after an hour. And if a jug of wine is poured into a jar of vinegar and no taste or smell is found, then drinking is permissible immediately. Chard (silq) and cabbage (saljam) cooked in ash of human waste (ramad al-adhrah) are impure according to Abu Yusuf. End quote.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This long passage from page 405 covers several important rulings and discussions. It begins with a discussion about passing wind while wearing trousers (sarawil) and then praying. Some scholars say it is invalid because wind has subtle particles that penetrate the fabric, but the relied-upon fatwa is that it is permissible, regardless of whether the trousers were wet or dry at the time. The text then digresses into a discussion about the ruling when both water and soil are pure or impure in determining the purity of mud; the preferred view is that if either is pure, the mud is pure, but there is debate. The author praises the view of Qadikhan and Abu al-Layth that the result follows from the worst premise (the more impure component determines the impurity). The text then returns to the main point: 'other than the last' refers to the four issues: bird not eaten, fish blood, saliva of donkey/mule, and urine splashes (like tip of needles); the fatwa is that praying with these impurities is permissible as long as the amount is less than a dirham. Then additional rulings: if one sees an impurity larger than a dirham on another's garment, one must inform them; the skin of a sheep's gall bladder (mirarah) is impure, and according to Muhammad the actual gall and urine of sheep are pure, but according to the two companions they are impure; the bladder of a sheep has the same ruling as its urine (thus impure). Then a rule about wine in vinegar: if a single drop of wine falls into a jar of vinegar, it becomes permissible only after an hour (presumably for the alcohol to transform), but if a whole jug of wine is poured and no taste/smell remains, it is immediately permissible. Finally, vegetables cooked in the ash of human waste are impure according to Abu Yusuf, implying impurity due to contact with najasah.

Key Terms
فسا (fasa): To pass wind audibly from the rectum, a form of minor impurity (hadath).
السراويل (sarawil): Trousers or pants, a garment covering the lower body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa in this passage, what is the ruling on praying after passing wind in trousers?
A) The prayer is invalid because wind carries particles
B) The prayer is valid, regardless of whether the trousers were wet or dry
C) The prayer is valid only if the trousers were dry
D) The prayer is valid only if the wind was silent

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that passing wind does not transfer impurity to clothing, and prayer remains valid.
